Validity of Nafl Fast for a Child Reaching Puberty or a Disbeliever Embracing Islam Before Midday
Ruling on nafl fast validity for child and disbeliever before midday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a nafl fast for a child who reaches puberty before midday and for a disbeliever who embraces Islam before midday, and explain the basis for the distinction.

Core Idea
A child's nafl fast becomes valid upon puberty, but a new Muslim's nafl fast is invalid that day because eligibility requires being Muslim at dawn.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition addresses a specific situation: what happens when a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever embraces Islam during the day before midday? Can they start a voluntary (nafl) fast on that same day? The answer depends on their eligibility for worship at the start of the day and the moment they become legally responsible (mukallaf). We will unpack the ruling and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: a child who reaches puberty (bulugh) before midday (zawal), and a disbeliever who embraces Islam (accepts faith) before midday.
2
State the ruling for each case
For the child: if he has not eaten or drunk since dawn, his intention for a nafl fast is valid and his fast is correct. For the disbeliever: even if he embraces Islam before midday and has not eaten, his intention for a nafl fast is not valid.
The ruling is clear: one is valid, the other is not.
3
Identify the condition for the child
The child must not have consumed anything since the start of the day (dawn). This condition ensures that he has not already broken a hypothetical fast. Since he was not legally responsible before puberty, his non-eating is not considered a fast, but after puberty, he can start a new intention.
If he had eaten, he would not be able to start a valid fast because the day is already broken.
4
Understand the rationale for the disbeliever
The text states that a disbeliever is not eligible (ahl) for nafl fast. The principle is that supererogatory acts require faith (iman) at the time of performance. Even though he embraces Islam before midday, his state before conversion makes him ineligible to start a nafl fast that day. The requirement of eligibility at the beginning of the day is implied.
This shows that eligibility for nafl acts is not acquired by conversion during the day; only obligatory acts might be affected differently.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Consider other scenarios: if the child reaches puberty after zawal, his intention is invalid because the day has passed. If a disbeliever embraces Islam at dawn exactly, the ruling might be different? The text only mentions before midday. This helps reinforce the importance of the moment of eligibility.
The Hanafi school has detailed rules for the exact moment of eligibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بچہ زوال سے پہلے بالغ ہو کر اگر نفل کی نیت کر لے تو نفلی روزہ درست ہو جائے گا بشرطیکہ صبح سے کچھ کھایا پیا نہ ہو۔ اور اگر زوال سے پہلے کا فر مسلمان ہو کر نفلی روزہ کی نیت کرلے تو اس کا نفلی روزہ درست نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ کافر نفلی روزہ کا اہل نہیں ہوتا البتہ نا بالغ بچ نفلی روزہ کا اہل ہوتا ہے۔
If a child reaches puberty before midday and intends a nafl fast, his nafl fast is valid, provided he has not eaten or drunk since dawn. And if a disbeliever becomes Muslim before midday and intends a nafl fast, his nafl fast is not valid, because a disbeliever is not eligible for nafl fast. However, a minor child is eligible for nafl fast.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about the validity of a nafl fast for a child who reaches puberty or a disbeliever who embraces Islam before midday (zawal). The ruling distinguishes between the two: a child, upon reaching puberty, becomes legally responsible (mukallaf) and can validly intend a nafl fast from that moment, provided he has not already broken his fast by eating or drinking earlier in the day. In contrast, a disbeliever who accepts Islam before midday cannot validly intend a nafl fast on that same day, because prior to his conversion, he was not eligible for acts of worship (including nafl) due to lack of iman. The reason is that a disbeliever is not considered a valid subject for supererogatory acts; eligibility (ahliyyah) for nafl requires faith. A child, even before puberty, is eligible for nafl because he is a Muslim, but his fast is not obligatory. The ruling is based on the principle that nafl acts require a valid intention at the time of initiation, and the person must be fit for legal address (mukhatab).

Key Terms
نفلی روزہ (nafli rozah): A supererogatory (voluntary) fast, not obligatory but rewarded.
بالغ (baligh): A person who has reached puberty and is thus legally responsible (mukallaf).
کافر (kafir): A disbeliever, one who does not believe in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a child reaches puberty before midday and intends a nafl fast, having eaten nothing since dawn?
A) The nafl fast is not valid because the child was not fasting before puberty.
B) The nafl fast is valid because the child is now a mukallaf and has not eaten.
C) The nafl fast is valid only if the child also intended to make up a missed Ramadan fast.
D) The nafl fast is invalid because nafl fasting requires intention before dawn.

Key Takeaway
A nafl fast is only valid if the person is eligible (Muslim) at the time of intention and, in the case of a child reaching puberty, must not have eaten/drunk since dawn.

Traveler's Intention to Break Fast and Return Before Midday: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Ruling on a traveler who intends to break fast but returns home before midday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a traveler who returns home before midday after intending to break fast, explain the a fortiori analogy (qiyas awla) used as proof, and identify when kaffarah is not obligatory due to legal doubt.

Core Idea
A traveler can validly begin a fast after intending to break it, if he returns home before midday; breaking it later incurs no kaffarah.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario: a traveler who initially intends not to fast, but before midday (zawal) returns to his hometown and changes his mind. The Hanafi school rules that his fast is valid and, in Ramadan, becomes obligatory. The proof is an a fortiori analogy: if a resident who travels cannot break his fast, a traveler who becomes resident is even more bound. Yet, if he breaks it, kaffarah is not required because of a legal doubt (shubhah) arising from the travel concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A traveler who intends to break his fast, then returns to the city before midday and intends to fast. The ruling: the fast is valid and, in Ramadan, obligatory (wajib). For voluntary fasts, it is mubah.
Note the change: from intending not to fast to intending to fast after return.
2
Locate the condition: return before midday
The key condition is that the return and intention must occur before zawal (midday). If after zawal, the fast is not valid because the time for intention has passed.
Zawal is the sun's meridian passage, a critical time marker in fasting rules.
3
Understand the legal reasoning: travel does not negate eligibility
The text states that travel does not remove the eligibility for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) nor the validity of commencing a fast (sihhat al-shuru'). Therefore, the traveler can validly start a fast even if he had earlier intended not to fast.
This principle supports the validity of the intention made after return.
4
Analyze the proof: qiyas awla (a fortiori analogy)
The proof is an analogy: if a resident who starts the day at home and then travels is not allowed to break his fast (preferring the residence side), then a traveler who becomes resident before midday is even more prohibited from breaking his fast. This is an a fortiori argument.
The qiyas is based on the stronger reason: residence trumps travel.
5
Note the exception: no kaffarah due to shubhah
Despite the obligation, if the traveler breaks the fast, he does not owe kaffarah (expiation). The reason is the presence of a legal doubt (shubhah) caused by the earlier travel concession.
Kaffarah is only for deliberate violation without any legal excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسافر نے افطار کی نیت کی پھر زوال سے پہلے شہر آ گیا پھر روزہ کی نیت کر لی یہ روزہ ہو جائے گا۔ وَإِذَا نَوَى الْمُسَافِرُ الْإِفْطَارَ ثُمَّ قَدِمَ الْمِصْرَ قَبْلَ الزَّوَالِ فَنَوَى الصَّوْمَ أَجْزَاهُ لِأَنَّ السَّفَرَ لَا يُنَا فِي أُهْلِيَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ وَلَا صِحَّةَ الشَّرُوعِ وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي رَمَضَانِ فَعَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَصُومَ لِزَوَالِ الْمُرَخَصِ فِي وَقْتِ النِّيَةِ الْاتَرَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ مُقِيِّمًا فِى أَوَّلِ الْيَوْمِ ثُمَّ سَافَرَ لَا يُبَاحُ لَهُ الْفِطْرُ تَرْجِيْحًا لِجَانِبِ الْإِقَامَةِ فَهُذَا أَوْلَى إِلَّا أَنَّهُ إِذَا أَفْطَرَ فِى الْمَسْأَلَتَيْنِ لا تَلْزَمُهُ الْكَفَّارَةُ لِقِيَامِ شُبْهَةِ الْمَيِّحِ.
A traveler intended to break fast, then before midday he came to the city, then intended fasting – this fast suffices. And when a traveler intends to break his fast, then returns to the city before midday, and then intends fasting, it suffices him, because travel does not negate eligibility for obligation nor validity of commencement. And if it is in Ramadan, he must fast due to the removal of the concession at the time of intention. Do you not see that if a resident were at the beginning of the day and then traveled, it is not permissible for him to break fast, preferring the side of residence; so this case is even more apt. Except that if he breaks fast in the two cases, kaffarah is not obligatory upon him due to the existence of the doubt of [the excuse].
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about a traveler who initially intends to break his fast (non-fasting) during a journey, but before midday (zawal) returns to his hometown and then intends to fast. The ruling states that this fast is valid and sufficient. The reason given is that travel does not remove either the eligibility for the obligation of the fast or the validity of commencing the fast. In other words, a traveler can validly begin a fast if he wishes, even if he had earlier intended not to fast. If this situation occurs in Ramadan, the fast becomes obligatory upon him because the concession (rukhsah) allowing travel-related breaking of fast is removed at the time of his intention (i.e., when he is no longer traveling). The text analogizes: if a resident starts the day and then travels, he is not allowed to break his fast (since the day began as resident); here, the traveler who becomes resident should even more so not break his fast. However, if he does break his fast despite this, he does not incur kaffarah (expiation) because there is a doubt (shubhah) due to the presence of the travel concession earlier. Kaffarah is only for intentional violation without legal doubt.

Key Terms
زوال (zawal): The time when the sun passes the meridian, i.e., midday, the time before which the ruling applies.
شبهة الميح (shubhat al-mayy?): Doubt due to an excuse (rukhsah) that makes kaffarah not obligatory. The term 'al-mayy' is likely a corruption of 'al-rukhsah' or similar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a traveler in Ramadan intends to break his fast in the morning, then returns home before midday and intends to fast, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is not valid because he already intended to break it.
B) The fast is valid and obligatory, and if he breaks it later, kaffarah is due.
C) The fast is valid and obligatory, but if he breaks it, kaffarah is not due due to doubt.
D) The fast is not valid because he must intend fasting from the night before.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a traveler who returns home before midday and intends to fast has a valid and, in Ramadan, obligatory fast; breaking it does not incur kaffarah due to legal doubt.

Unconsciousness in Ramadan: Qada for the First Day vs. Subsequent Days
Ruling on qada for day unconsciousness begins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada when a person becomes unconscious during Ramadan and distinguish between the day of onset and subsequent days.

Core Idea
The first day of unconsciousness requires no qada; subsequent days do.

This lesson examines a scenario where a fasting person becomes unconscious during the day in Ramadan. The Hanafi school differentiates between the day unconsciousness begins and later days. Understanding this nuance prevents unnecessary qada and preserves the validity of the fast when intention was present at the start.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Legal Category
The act is becoming unconscious (ighma') while in a state of fasting during Ramadan. The question is whether qada is required for the day unconsciousness occurred and for subsequent days.
This is a ruling with a proof based on talil (causal reasoning).
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
For the day on which unconsciousness begins, qada is NOT required. For days after, qada IS required.
That is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Examine the Reasoning (Talil)
The fast is considered valid for the day of onset because the person had intention (niyyah) at the start—presumed from the apparent condition (zahir al-hal) of a Muslim during Ramadan—and performed abstinence (imsak) for the conscious part. Subsequent days lack any intention from the start, so the fast is invalid.
Intention is a condition for fasting; without it, the fast is not valid.
4
Apply the Ruling
Calculate the number of qada days: count all full days of unconsciousness except the first partial day where unconsciousness began.
Example: Unconscious from 10 AM on the 1st of Ramadan until after Maghrib on the 3rd – qada for the 2nd and 3rd only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رمضان میں بے ہوشی طاری ہو گئی جس دن بے ہوشی طاری ہوئی اس دن کی قضا لازم نہیں وَمَنْ أُعْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ فِى رَمَضَانَ لَمْ يَقْضِ الْيَوْمَ الَّذِى حَدَثَ فِيهِ الْإِعْمَاءُ لِوُجُودِ الصَّوْمِ فِيهِ وَهُوَ الْإِمْسَاكُ الْمَقْرُونُ بِالنِّيَّةِ إِذِ الظَّاهِرُ وُجُودُهَا مِنْهُ وَقَضَى مَا بَعْدَهُ لِإِنْعِدَامِ النِّيَّةِ ترجمہ اور جس شخص پر رمضان میں بے ہوشی طاری ہوئی تو وہ اس روزے کی قضاء نہ کرے جس میں بے ہوشی شروع ہوئی ہے کیونکہ اس دن روزہ پایا گیا یعنی مفطرات سے پرہیز نیت کے ساتھ پایا گیا ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر حال اس شخص سے نیت ہے اور اس دن سے بعد والے دنوں کی قضاء کرے کیونکہ نیت معدوم ہے۔
Unconsciousness occurred in Ramadan. The day on which unconsciousness occurred, its qada is not obligatory. And whoever is overtaken by unconsciousness in Ramadan does not make qada for the day in which the unconsciousness occurred, due to the existence of fasting in it, which is abstinence combined with intention, since the apparent condition of the person is that intention exists from him. And he makes qada for the days after it due to the absence of intention.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage addresses a person who becomes unconscious (ighma') during daylight hours in Ramadan. The ruling distinguishes between the day of onset and subsequent days. For the day on which unconsciousness begins, qada (make-up fast) is not required. The reasoning is that the fast of that day is considered to have been observed. This is because the fast consists of abstinence (imsak) from things that break the fast, accompanied by intention (niyyah). Since the person was conscious at the start of the day and presumably had the habitual intention of a Muslim to fast during Ramadan, the intention is considered present based on the apparent condition (zahir al-hal). Therefore, the abstinence during the conscious part before unconsciousness and the continuation of abstinence (even unconsciously) counts as a valid fast. However, for the subsequent days (from the next day onward), the person was unconscious from the beginning of those days, so there is no intention, and thus qada is required for those days. The principle: the fast of the first day is valid because intention is presumed from the preceding night or dawn; for later days, no intention was made.

Key Terms
بے ہوشی (إغماء) (behoshi (ighma')): Unconsciousness, a state where a person is unaware and unable to intend acts of worship.
ظاہر حال (zahir al-hal): The apparent condition or presumptive state; here, the assumption that a Muslim would have intended to fast on a Ramadan day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person becomes unconscious at 10 AM on the first day of Ramadan and remains unconscious for three full days, how many days must he make up?
A) He must make up all three days because unconsciousness breaks the fast.
B) He must make up only the first day because it was partially valid.
C) He must make up the two days after the first day, but not the first day.
D) He must make up no days because he was unconscious.

Key Takeaway
The day unconsciousness begins in Ramadan requires no qada because intention is presumed; subsequent days require qada due to absence of intention.

Unconsciousness from First Night of Ramadan: Hanafi vs Maliki on Intention
Ruling on qada for unconsciousness from first night; Hanafi vs Maliki

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on qada for an unconscious person during Ramadan and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument regarding intention.

Core Idea
Fasting each day is a separate act of worship — a single intention does not cover the entire month.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a person who becomes unconscious from the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious for the entire month. The Hanafi and Maliki schools differ on whether qada is required. The dispute hinges on the nature of intention in Ramadan fasting — is one intention sufficient for the whole month, or must each day have its own?

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: a person who becomes unconscious from the first night must make qada for all days except the day of that first night (i.e., 29 days).
The first day is considered valid due to intention formed before unconsciousness.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Malik holds that no qada is required for the days after the first night. The entire month's fast is fulfilled with a single intention at the beginning.
Malik's position is based on the analogy to i'tikaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Malik argues that Ramadan is a single act of worship, like i'tikaf, so one intention suffices. Therefore, unconsciousness does not break the month's fast.
The analogy relies on the continuous nature of the act.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school refutes the analogy: fasting each day is a separate act of worship, because the night between days is not part of the fasting time. I'tikaf is continuous; fasting is not.
Thus, each day requires its own intention; unconsciousness prevents intention for subsequent days.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi requires qada for 29 days; Maliki requires no qada at all. The dispute has significant impact on the person's obligation.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ أُعمِيَ عَلَيْهِ أَوَّلَ لَيْلَةٍ مِنْهُ قَضاهُ كُلَّهُ غَيْرَ يَوْمٍ تِلْكَ اللَّيْلَةِ لِمَا قُلْنَا وَقَالَ مَالِكَ لَا يَقْضِي مَا بَعْدَهُ لِأَنَّ صَوْمَ رَمَضَانَ عَنْهُ يَتَادَّى بِنَيَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ بِمَنْزَلَةِ الْإِعْتِكَافِ وَ عِندَنَا لَا بُدَّ مِنَ النّيَّةِ لِكُلِّ يَوْمٍ لِأَنَّهَا عِبَادَاتٌ مُتَفَرٍ قَةٌ لِأنَّه يَتَخَلَّلُ بَيْنَ كُلِّ يَوْمَيْنِ مَا لَيْسَ بِزَمَانِ لِهَذِهِ الْعِبَادَةِ بِخِلَافِ الْإِعْتِكَافِ
And if he is overtaken by unconsciousness on the first night of it, he makes qada for all of it except the day of that night, for what we have said. And Malik said: He does not make qada for what is after it, because the fast of Ramadan is fulfilled from him with a single intention, like the case of i'tikaf. And according to us, an intention is necessary for every day, because they are separate acts of worship, since between every two days there intervenes that which is not a time for this worship, unlike i'tikaf.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and a dispute with Imam Malik regarding a person who becomes unconscious (aghmiya 'alayhi) from the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious for the entire month. The Hanafi position is that he must make qada (make up) the entire month except for the day of that first night (i.e., the first day is considered valid and not qada). The reasoning is that the first day was valid because the intention was formed before unconsciousness (as per the previous ruling). However, for subsequent days, intention is absent, so qada is required. Imam Malik holds a different view: he says no qada is required for the days after the first night because, according to Maliki fiqh, a single intention at the beginning of Ramadan suffices for the entire month, similar to i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque) where one intention covers the entire period. The Hanafi response to Malik is that fasting each day is a separate act of worship ('ibadat mutafarriqah). Evidence: between each two days there is an intervening time (the night) that is not part of the fasting time. Unlike i'tikaf, which is a continuous period. Therefore, each day requires its own intention. Thus, if intention is absent for subsequent days due to unconsciousness, those days must be made up.

Key Terms
اعتکاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in the mosque for worship, used here as an analogy by Malik.
عبارات متفرقہ (ibadat mutafarriqah): Separate acts of worship, each independent from the others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person becomes unconscious from the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious for the entire month, how many days must he make up?
A) All 30 days.
B) 29 days (all except the first day).
C) None, because one intention suffices for the month.
D) Only the last day because it was broken by the end of month.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi reliance on the separateness of each day's fast leads to qada for all days except the first when unconscious from night one.

Unconsciousness During Ramadan: Which Days Require Qada?
Ruling on qada for days with unconsciousness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a Muslim who becomes unconscious during Ramadan and explain why the first day is valid while subsequent days require qada.

Core Idea
Intention for the first day is presumed; unconsciousness after that prevents intention, making later days invalid.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a Muslim who becomes unconscious after dawn in Ramadan and remains so for several days. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the day unconsciousness begins and the following days based on the presence or absence of intention for fasting. The reasoning relies on the principle of 'zahir al-hal' (apparent state) to infer intention for the first day. Understanding this ruling ensures proper observance of qada duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the problem
The act is fasting in Ramadan; the problem is unconsciousness occurring after dawn and lasting multiple days.
2
State the ruling
No qada for the day unconsciousness began; qada is wajib for all subsequent days.
3
Examine the proof: role of intention
Intention (niyyah) is required for each day's fast. The ruling hinges on whether intention was present at the start of each day.
4
Understand the basis: zahir al-hal
For the first day, the apparent state of a Muslim in Ramadan is that he intended the fast (e.g., he ate suhur and avoided nullifiers). This presumption validates the first day.
5
Apply to later days
Unconsciousness prevents the formation of intention for later days, so those fasts are invalid and require qada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أُعْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ فِى رَمْضَانَ لَمْ يَقْضِ الْيَوْمَ الَّذِى حَدَثَ فِيهِ الْإِعْمَاءُ لِوُجُودِ الْقَوْمِ فِيهِ وَهُوَ الْإِمْسَاك الْمَقْرُونُ بِالنِّيَةِ إِذِ الظَّاهِرُ وُجُودُهَا مِنْهُ وَ قَضَى مَا بَعْدَهُ لِإِنْعِدَامِ النِّيَةِ
ترجمہ اور جس شخص پر رمضان میں بے ہوشی طاری ہوئی تو وہ اس روزے کی قضاء نہ کرے جس میں بے ہوشی شروع ہوئی ہے کیونکہ اس دن روزہ پایا گیا یعنی مفطرات سے بار رہنا نیت کے ساتھ پایا گیا ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر حال اس شخص سے نیت ہے اور اس دن سے بعد والے دنوں کی قضاء کرے کیونکہ نیت معدوم ہے۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر رمضان کے مہینہ میں طلوع فجر کے بعد کسی پر بے ہوشی طاری ہوگئی اور کئی روز تک بے ہوش رہا تو جس دن بے ہوشی شروع ہوتی ہے اس دن کی قضا تو نہ کرے البتہ اس کے بعد کے دنوں کی قضاء کرنا واجب ہوگا۔ اس دن کی قضا ، تو اس لئے واجب نہیں ہے کہ اس دن کا روزہ پایا گیا۔ اس طور پر کہ یہ شخص روزے کی نیت کے ساتھ مفطرات صوم سے باز رہا اور نیت کا تحقق اس طور پر پر ہوا کہ یہ مشخص مسلمان ہے اور رمضان کی راتوں میں مسلمان کا ظاہر حال یہ ہے کہ اس کی کوئی رات بغیر نیت کے نہیں گذرتی پس جب بمقتضائے ظاہر حال نیت کے ساتھ مفطرات سے رکنا پایا گیا تو روزہ پایا گیا ۔ اور جب اس دن کا روزہ پایا گیا تو اس کی قضاء کرنے کی چنداں ضرورت نہیں ہے اور اس سے بعد کے دنوں میں چونکہ نیت نہیں پائی گئی اس لئے ان دنوں میں مفطرات سے رکنا روزہ شمار نہ ہوگا۔ اور نیت اس لئے نہیں پائی گئی کہ انما یعنی بے ہوشی طاری ہو نا مانع نیت ہے۔
And whoever becomes unconscious in Ramadan does not make up the day in which the unconsciousness occurred because the fast is found in it, i.e., abstention from nullifiers connected with intention, since the apparent state is that it exists from him. And he makes up what is after it due to the absence of intention. Explanation: If someone becomes unconscious after dawn in Ramadan and remains unconscious for several days, he does not make up the day on which unconsciousness began, but he must make up the days after it. He does not make up that day because the fast of that day is found, in the sense that he abstained from the nullifiers of fasting with the intention of fasting, and the intention is realized because he is a specific Muslim, and the apparent state of Muslims during Ramadan nights is that no night passes without intention. So when, by the requirement of apparent state, abstention with intention is found, the fast is found. And when the fast of that day is found, there is no need to make it up. As for the days after it, since intention is not found, abstention from nullifiers in those days does not count as fasting. And intention is not found because unconsciousness is a preventer of intention.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof addressing the case of a person who becomes unconscious during the day in Ramadan. It states that the day on which unconsciousness begins does not require qada (make-up) because that day's fast is considered valid. The reasoning is that the person had the intention for fasting (since he is a Muslim and it is Ramadan, the apparent state is that he intended the fast), and he abstained from nullifiers until unconsciousness struck. Since the fast was validly begun, it is counted. For the subsequent days of unconsciousness, since no intention was formed (unconsciousness prevents intention), those days must be made up. The commentary explains that intention is assumed for the first day based on the apparent state (zahir al-hal) of Muslims in Ramadan. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and relies on the principle that intention is a prerequisite for each day's fast.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Unconsciousness; a state that prevents the formation of intention for fasting.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Making up a missed obligatory fast after Ramadan.
نية (niyyah): Intention for fasting, required for each day's fast in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person becomes unconscious after dawn in Ramadan and remains unconscious for two days, which days must he make up?
A) Neither day
B) Only the first day
C) Only the second day
D) Both days

Key Takeaway
The first day of unconsciousness is valid due to presumed intention; later days require qada because intention is impossible.

Hanafi vs Maliki on Unconsciousness and Ramadan Qada
Dispute with Malik on qada for entire month if unconscious from first night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires qada for all days except the first when a person becomes unconscious on the first night of Ramadan, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
Each day of Ramadan fasting is a separate worship requiring its own intention; the intervening night breaks continuity, unlike i'tikaf.

In this lesson, we examine a classical dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding the obligation of qada for someone who becomes unconscious on the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious the entire month. The disagreement centers on whether all Ramadan fasts can be performed with a single intention (as in i'tikaf) or require separate intentions for each day. By understanding the Hanafi reasoning, you will grasp a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: the necessity of renewing intention for each day of Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school rule in this case? Identify the act (qada) and its ruling (wajib) and which days are included.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school or imam disagrees? What is their position? (Imam Malik holds no qada is required.)
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Maliki evidence? (Analogy to i'tikaf: a single intention suffices for the entire month.)
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the Maliki argument? (Each day is a separate worship because night intervenes; i'tikaf includes night, fasting excludes it.)
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of the disagreement? (Hanafi: qada of many days; Maliki: no qada.)

نہیں ہے۔
And if he becomes unconscious on the first night of it, he makes up all of it except the day of that night, for the reason we mentioned. And Malik said: He does not make up what is after it because the fast of Ramadan is performed with a single intention, like i'tikaf. According to us, intention is necessary for every day because they are separate acts of worship because between every two days there is what is not the time for this worship, unlike i'tikaf. Explanation: If someone becomes unconscious on the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious the entire month, qada is wajib for the entire month except the first day, because after sighting the crescent, the apparent state is that he intended the first fast. And when he intended the first fast, that fast is valid and no qada is required. As for the subsequent days, since intention is not found, qada is wajib. But if he became unconscious before sighting the crescent, then qada for the first day is also wajib because intention before sighting is not valid. Imam Malik says: If he becomes unconscious on the first night and remains unconscious the whole month, no qada is required at all; the entire month's fasts are considered valid. His proof is that all Ramadan fasts are performed with a single intention, like i'tikaf. According to us, separate intention for each day is necessary because each day's fast is a separate act of worship, as seen when one day's fast becomes invalid, it does not affect others. Also, between two days there is night, which is not the time for fasting, so they are disconnected. Thus each day is a separate worship, requiring its own intention, unlike i'tikaf where day and night are all the time of i'tikaf, so a single intention suffices.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding a person who becomes unconscious on the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious the entire month. The Hanafi position is that he must make up all days except the first day (if he saw the crescent), because the first day's fast is valid based on the apparent assumption of intention, but subsequent days lack intention. Imam Malik holds that no qada is required at all, because in his view a single intention for the entire month suffices, like i'tikaf. The Hanafi rebuttal explains that each day's fast is a separate act of worship (ibadah mutafarriqah), unlike i'tikaf which is continuous. The passage also notes an exception: if the person became unconscious before sighting the crescent, even the first day requires qada because intention before sighting is not valid in Hanafi fiqh. The lengthy commentary clarifies the reasoning behind the Hanafi position, emphasizing the need for a separate intention for each day and rejecting analogy to i'tikaf because the night intervenes and is not part of the fasting time.

Key Terms
اعتکاف (iʿtikāf): Seclusion in the mosque for worship, considered a single continuous act.
نية (niyyah): Intention for fasting, required for each day in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) None
B) All days
C) All days except the first
D) Only the first day

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires separate intention for each day of Ramadan because each fast is a separate act of worship, making qada necessary for days without intention.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Ruling on Qada for Unconsciousness Throughout Ramadan
Ruling with rationale for qada of missed fasts due to unconsciousness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for a person unconscious the entire Ramadan and explain the legal reasoning (talil) that distinguishes it from insanity.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness excuses delay but not obligation — qada is wajib.

This lesson examines a specific ruling regarding a person who remains unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan. While it may seem that such a severe condition would exempt one from fasting, the Hanafi school, based on a talil (rationale), holds that the obligation is merely deferred. We will explore the reasoning behind this and clarify the distinction between unconsciousness and insanity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The scenario is a person unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan. The ruling is that he must make up (qada) all the missed days. This is a wajib obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is a talil (rational analogy): unconsciousness is a type of sickness. The author likens it to illness.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Illness is an excuse that allows one to delay the fast but does not drop the obligation entirely. Similarly, unconsciousness weakens the body but does not remove the intellect, so it only excuses delay.
4
Distinguish from insanity
Insanity (junun) removes the intellect entirely and thus drops the obligation. Unconsciousness leaves the intellect intact, so the obligation remains.
5
Note the authority
This is the zahir al-riwayah position, and it is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أُعْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ فِي رَمَضَانَ كُلّهِ قَضَاهُ لِأَنَّهُ نَوعُ مَرَض يُضْعِفُ الْقَوِيَّ وَلَا يُزِيلُ الْحِجَى فَيَصِيرُ عُذُرًا فِي التاخير لا فِي الْإِسْقَاطِ
پورا رمضان بے ہوشی طاری رہی تو پورے رمضان کی قضا کرے
And whoever becomes unconscious in the entire Ramadan makes it up because it is a type of sickness that weakens the strong and does not remove intellect, so it becomes an excuse for delay, not for dropping (the obligation).
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling that if a person is unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan, he must make up all the fasting days later. The reasoning is that unconsciousness (ighma') is a type of illness that weakens the body but does not remove the intellect (hija). Therefore, it is considered a valid excuse for delaying the fasts, but it does not remove the obligation entirely. Unlike insanity (junun) which removes obligation, unconsciousness is temporary and does not lift the requirement of qada. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that qada is wajib for missed Ramadan fasts due to a valid excuse.

Key Terms
حجی (ḥijā): Intellect or mental faculty that remains intact during unconsciousness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) No fasts are required at all
B) He must make up all the days
C) He must only make up the first day
D) He must only make up the last day

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness for an entire Ramadan does not remove the obligation to fast; it only excuses delay, so qada of all days is wajib.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Why an Unconscious Person Must Make Up Ramadan Fasts
Debate on unconsciousness and intention with Maliki and Hasan al-Basri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a person unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan must make up the fasts (qada), and refute the opposing arguments of Imam Malik and Hasan al-Basri.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness delays the obligation of fasting; it does not drop it. Separate intention is required for each day.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a person who remains unconscious (maghmi 'alayhi) throughout Ramadan. The Hanafis maintain that qada is wajib for all missed fasts, contrary to the Maliki school and Hasan al-Basri. We will analyze the proofs from the text, including the nature of unconsciousness as a covering of intellect, not its destruction, and the requirement of separate intention for each day's fast.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling: the unconscious person must make up all fasts of Ramadan. This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The text explicitly states that qada is wajib for the entire month.
2
Understand the Opposing Views
Imam Malik holds that one intention suffices for the whole month, so no qada is required. Hasan al-Basri argues that the obligation never arose because the cause (witnessing the month) was not present due to lack of intellect.
Both opponents use the premise that the person was never properly addressed by the law.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi rebuttal has two parts: (a) each day's fast is an independent act of worship requiring its own intention, so one intention cannot cover the whole month; (b) unconsciousness covers the intellect but does not destroy it, as evidenced by the Prophet's experience of ighma while protected from madness. Therefore, the obligation remains but performance is delayed.
The Prophet's ighma is cited to show that intellect is not removed.
4
Evaluate the Role of Intention
Intention before the moon sighting is invalid, so if unconsciousness began before sighting, even the first day must be made up. For subsequent days, each day requires its own intention, and since unconsciousness prevented that, qada is needed.
This refutes Malik's argument that one intention at the start suffices.
5
Derive the Practical Ruling
The Hanafi position is that qada is wajib for all days of unconsciousness. The opposing views are rejected because they incorrectly treat unconsciousness like insanity or conflate separate acts of worship.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis require qada; Malik and Hasan do not.

پورا رمضان بے ہوشی طاری رہی تو پورے رمضان کی قضا کرے
وَمَنْ أُعْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ فِي رَمَضَانَ كُلّهِ قَضَاهُ لِأَنَّهُ نَوعُ مَرَض يُضْعِفُ الْقَوِيَّ وَلَا يُزِيلُ الْحِجَى فَيَصِيرُ عُذُرًا فِي التاخير لا فِي الْإِسْقَاطِ

وَ مَنْ جُنَّ فِي رَمَضَانَ كُلَّه لَمْ يَقضِهِ خِلَاقًا لِمَالِكِ وَ هُوَ يَعْتَبِرُهُ بِالْإِغْمَاءِ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْمُسْقِطَ هُوَ الْخَرَجُ وَالْإِعْمَاء لا يَسْتَوْعِبُ الشَّهْرَ عَادَةً فَلَا حَرَجَ وَالْجُنُونُ يَسْتَوْعِبُه فَيَتَحَقَقُ الحَرَج
ترجمہ اور جو شخص پورے رمضان مجنون رہا تو وہ اس کی قضاء نہ کرے گا۔ امام مالک کا اختلاف ہے۔ امام مالک جنون کو انماء پر قیاس کرتے ہیں اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ ساقط کرنے والا تو حرج ہے اور انماء عادہ پور لے کا استیعاب نہیں کرتا لہذا حرج بھی نہیں ہے اور جنون پورے ماہ کا استیعاب کر لیتا ہے اس لئے حرج متحقق ہو جائے گا۔
تشریح صاحب کفا یہ لکھتے ہیں کہ اعذار چار قسم کے ہوتے ہیں، ایک وہ جو عادہ ایک دن رات تک ممتد نہ ہوتا ہو جیسے نوم اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس کی وجہ سے کوئی عبادت ساقہ نہیں ہوتی کیونکہ یہ عذر موجب حرج نہیں ہوتا ۔ ۲) دوسرے وہ عذر جو پیدائش اور خلقی طور پر متد ہوتا ہے جیسے عدم بلوغ کا زمانہ اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ یہ تمام عبادت کو ساقط کر دیتا ہے کیونکہ یہ موجب حرج ہے پس حرج دور کرنے کے لئے بچہ تمام عبادتیں ساقط ہو گئیں۔ (۳) تیسرے وہ عذر جو عادۃ ایک نماز کے وقت کی مقدار تو ممتد ہوتا ہے لیکن روزے کے وقت کی مقدار ممتد نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسے اغماء اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک دن رات سے زائد ہو گیا رفع حرج کے لئے اس کو عذر قرار دیا جائے گا۔ یعنی اگر اغماء کی وجہ سے چھ نمازیں فوت
ہو جائیں تو ان کی قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی یعنی حرج دور کرنے کے لئے قضاء ساقط ہوگئی۔
For the entire month except the first day, making up is obligatory because after the moon of Ramadan is sighted, the apparent state of a Muslim is that he must have intended the first fast. And when he intended the first fast, that fast is legally valid and its qada is not obligatory, and since he did not intend the subsequent fasts, their qada is obligatory. Yes, if this person became unconscious before the moon was sighted, then qada of the first fast is also obligatory because intention before sighting is not valid. Imam Malik says: if unconsciousness occurred on the first night and he remained unconscious the whole month and refrained from nullifiers, then no qada is obligatory upon him at all; rather the entire month's fasts are legally valid. Imam Malik's proof is that all fasts of Ramadan can be performed with one intention; it is not necessary to make a separate intention for each fast, just as for a ten-day i'tikaf one intention is sufficient; it is not necessary to intend each day's i'tikaf. In any case, according to Malik, one intention suffices for all Ramadan fasts, and on the first night, by his evident state, intention existed as the Hanafis also hold, so for the whole Ramadan intention-based abstention occurred, and when that is the case, all Ramadan fasts are performed, so how can qada be obligatory? According to us, it is necessary to make a separate intention for each day because each day's fast is a separate act of worship. Observe that if one day's fast becomes invalid, the remaining days' fasts do not become invalid. Similarly, if capacity is absent on some days, it does not necessitate that capacity is absent on the remaining days. Rather, it is possible that in some days of Ramadan a person is not eligible for fasting due to disbelief, but when he accepts Islam, eligibility is established for the remaining days. The conclusion is that not all Ramadan fasts are one act of worship; rather each day's fast is a separate act of worship, just as each prayer is a separate act of worship. Ramadan fasts are separate acts of worship because between two fasts there is a night interval that is not a time for the worship of fasting, so the time of one fast is not connected to another fast. And when there is no connection between two fasts, they count as two acts of worship, not one. Otherwise, if all fasts were considered one act of worship, it would necessitate an interval within one act of worship that is not time for that worship, which is contrary to the nature of worship. Therefore we say that all fasts are not one act of worship as Malik says; rather each fast is a separate act of worship, and when each fast is a separate act of worship, it is necessary to make a separate intention for each fast, contrary to i'tikaf where night and day are all time for i'tikaf. So i'tikaf entirely counts as one act of worship, and one intention suffices for it, but it is not correct to analogize fasts to i'tikaf.
If unconsciousness persisted throughout Ramadan, he makes up the whole month.
"And whoever is unconscious throughout the whole of Ramadan, he makes it up because it is a type of illness that weakens strength but does not remove intellect, so it becomes an excuse for delay, not for dropping."
Translation: And the person upon whom unconsciousness persisted throughout the whole of Ramadan, he makes up the whole of Ramadan because ighma is a type of illness that weakens strength and does not remove intellect. Therefore ighma is considered an excuse for delaying the fast but not an excuse for dropping it. Explanation of the issue: If a person remained unconscious the whole month of Ramadan, he will make up the whole Ramadan. According to Hasan al-Basri, such a person is not obliged to make up. Hasan al-Basri's proof is that the cause for obligation of performing the fast is the witnessing of the month, and this was not found in his case because before the commencement of Ramadan, his intellect was removed due to unconsciousness, and without intellect, no one is addressed by the rulings of Shari'ah. So when this person was no longer addressed by the Law, performance was not obligatory upon him, and when performance was not obligatory, how can qada be obligatory? Our proof: Before hearing, note that in the case of ighma, the intellect is covered but not removed or destroyed. In the state of insanity, the intellect is destroyed. You know that during his illness, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) experienced ighma, although he was protected from loss of intellect, as Allah says about him: "You are not, by the favor of your Lord, mad." So when it is established that ighma weakens strength but does not destroy the intellect, rather the intellect remains but is covered for some time as it is covered in sleep, therefore due to ighma the fast can be delayed but cannot be dropped. And when the fast is not dropped from the unconscious person, qada is necessarily obligatory upon him.
If one remained insane for the whole of Ramadan, qada is not obligatory... Imam Malik's viewpoint.
"And whoever is insane for the whole of Ramadan does not make it up, contrary to Malik, who considers it analogous to ighma. Our proof is that what drops is hardship, and ighma usually does not cover the whole month, so there is no hardship; insanity covers the whole month, so hardship is realized."
Translation: And the person who remained insane for the whole of Ramadan will not make it up. Imam Malik disagrees. Imam Malik analogizes insanity to ighma. Our proof is that what causes dropping is hardship, and ighma does not usually cover the whole month, so there is no hardship; insanity covers the whole month, so hardship is realized.
Explanation: The author of Kifayah writes that excuses are of four types: First, those that usually do not extend to a day and night, like sleep; its ruling is that no act of worship is dropped due to it because this excuse does not cause hardship. Second, those that are innate and congenital, like the period of non-bulugh; its ruling is that it drops all acts of worship because it causes hardship, so to remove hardship, all acts of worship are dropped for the child. Third, those that usually extend to the duration of one prayer time but not to the duration of fasting, like ighma; its ruling is that if it exceeds a day and night, it will be considered an excuse for removal of hardship, i.e., if six prayers are missed due to ighma, their qada is not obligatory — qada is dropped to remove hardship.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf Ul Hadaya is a detailed commentary on the rulings for a person who is unconscious (maghmi 'alayhi) or insane (majnun) during the entire month of Ramadan. The Hanafi position is that unconsciousness does not remove the obligation of fasting; rather it only allows delay. Therefore, one who is unconscious for the entire month must make up the fasts (qada). In contrast, insanity for the entire month does remove the obligation, so no qada is required. The passage begins by addressing the issue of intention (niyyah) for each day, establishing that each day's fast is an independent act of worship requiring its own intention. This refutes Imam Malik's view that one intention suffices for the whole month. The author then presents the matn (core text) giving the ruling on ighma, followed by a debate with Imam Malik (and also Hasan al-Basri) who argue that no qada is required because the cause of obligation (witnessing the moon) is not present. The Hanafi rebuttal explains that in ighma the intellect is merely covered, not destroyed, as evidenced by the Prophet's experience of ighma while ill. Therefore the obligation remains, but performance is delayed. For insanity (junun), the intellect is completely removed, and since insanity typically lasts the entire month, hardship (haraj) would result if qada were required; hence it is dropped. The passage concludes with a classification of four types of excuses from the Kifayah, showing how each affects the obligation of worship.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighma'): Unconsciousness where intellect is covered but not destroyed; a type of illness that weakens strength.
جنون (junun): Insanity where intellect is destroyed; it removes legal responsibility.
حرج (haraj): Hardship that justifies dropping an obligation; the key factor distinguishing ighma from junun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a person who remains unconscious (maghmi 'alayhi) for the entire month of Ramadan?
A) He must make up all the fasts (qada).
B) He does not need to make up any fasts.
C) He only needs to make up the first day.
D) He must give fidya instead of fasting.

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness is a legitimate excuse for delaying the fast, but not for dropping the obligation; therefore, qada is wajib.

The Obligation of Qada for Unconsciousness Throughout Ramadan: Hanafi Proof from Quran 68:2
Ruling on make-up for unconsciousness throughout Ramadan with opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for a person unconscious the entire Ramadan and explain the proof from Quran 68:2 by distinguishing ighma' from junun.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness does not remove legal capacity; make-up of missed fasts remains obligatory.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: unconsciousness (ighma') versus insanity (junun). While insanity for the entire month drops the obligation, unconsciousness only delays it. The proof lies in the Quranic verse that negates madness from the Prophet during his illness, showing intellect remains. We will explore the reasoning and the opposing view of Hasan al-Basri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what act is under discussion (a person unconscious for the entire Ramadan) and state the Hanafi ruling (qada is obligatory).
The ruling is that make-up of all missed fasts is fard.
2
State the Hanafi reasoning
Unconsciousness (ighma') is like an illness; it weakens faculties but does not destroy intellect, so the obligation remains but is delayed.
This distinguishes ighma' from junun where intellect is completely destroyed.
3
Examine Hasan al-Basri's opposing view and his proof
He argues that without intellect the person is not addressed by Sharia, so no obligation to fast arose, hence no qada.
His basis: the cause (sighting of the month) did not apply to the person due to lack of intellect.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response using Quran 68:2 and the analogy to sleep
The verse negates madness from the Prophet despite his unconsciousness, proving intellect remains; analogy to sleep shows intellect is veiled, not removed.
Thus the person remains mukallaf and qada is required.
5
Derive the final ruling and explain why qada is fard
Since the fast was not dropped, it must be made up; the original obligation was fard, so qada is fard.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب الصوم
۲۵۰
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد سوم
ترجمہ اور جس شخص پر پورے رمضان میں بے ہوشی طاری رہی وہ پورے رمضان کی قضا کرے کیونکہ اغماء ایک قسم کا مرض ہے جو قوتوں کو ضعیف کر دیتا ہے اور عقل کو زائل نہیں کرتا۔ اس لئے انماء روزہ کو مؤخر کرنے میں عذر شمار ہوگا لیکن ساقط کرنے میں عذر شمار نہیں ہوگا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص پورے ماہ رمضان بے ہوش رہا تو پورے رمضان کی قضاء کرے گا حسن بصری کے نزدیک ایسے لہ شخص پر قضا واجب نہیں ہے حسن بصری کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وجوب ادائے صوم کا سبب شہور شہر ہے اور یہ اس کے حق میں پایا نہیں گیا کیونکہ رمضان شروع ہونے سے پہلے اعماء کی وجہ سے اس کی عقل زائل ہوگئی ہے اور بغیر عقل کے کوئی شخص احکام شرع کا مخاطب نہیں ہوتا۔ پس جب یہ شخص شریعت کا مخاطب ہی نہ رہا تو اس پر ادا لازم نہ ہوئی اور جب ادا لازم نہ ہوئی تو قضا کہاں سے واجب ہوگی۔ ہماری دلیل سماعت کرنے سے پہلے یہ بات ذہن نشین کر لیجئے کہ اعماء کی صورت میں عقل مستور تو ہوتی ہے لیکن مسلوب اور زائل نہیں ہوتی ۔ اور جنون کی حالت میں عقل زائل ہو جاتی ہے چنانچہ آپ کو معلوم ہوگا کہ مرض کے زمانہ میں رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم پر اغماء طاری ہوا حالانکہ آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم زوال عقل سے معصوم تھے جیسا کہ آپ کے بارے میں حق جل مجدہ کا ارشاد ہے ما انتَ بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ بمَجْنُون پس جب یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ اغماء قومی کو کمزور تو کر دیتا ہے مگر عقل زائل نہیں کرتا بلکہ معقل باقی رہتی ہے البتہ کچھ وقت کے لئے مستور ہو جاتی ہے جیسا کہ نیند کی حالت میں عقل مستور ہو جاتی ہے۔ اس لئے انماء کی وجہ سے روزہ کو موخر تو کیا جا سکتا ہے مگر ساقط نہیں کیا جا سکتا۔ اور جب معمی علیہ سے روزہ ساقط نہیں ہوا تو اس پر لازما قضا واجب ہوگی ۔
The Book of Fasting. Explanation: A person who remained unconscious for the entire Ramadan should make up the entire Ramadan because unconsciousness is a type of illness that weakens the faculties but does not remove intellect. Therefore unconsciousness is an excuse for delaying the fast but not for dropping it. Explanation of the issue: If someone remains unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan, he will make up the entire Ramadan. According to Hasan al-Basri, such a person is not obligated to make up. His proof: The cause of the obligation to perform the fast is the sighting of the month, and this did not occur for him because before the start of Ramadan, due to unconsciousness, his intellect had ceased, and without intellect, a person is not addressed by the rulings of Sharia. Since this person was not addressed by Sharia, performance was not obligatory, and when performance is not obligatory, how can make-up be obligatory? Our proof: Before listening, note that in the case of unconsciousness, the intellect is veiled but not removed or destroyed. In madness, the intellect is destroyed. As you know, during the time of illness, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) experienced unconsciousness, even though he was protected from loss of intellect. Allah says about him: 'You are not, by the grace of your Lord, mad.' Since it is established that unconsciousness weakens the faculties but does not destroy the intellect, rather the intellect remains, but is veiled for a time like in sleep, therefore the fast can be delayed due to unconsciousness but cannot be dropped. And when the fast is not dropped from the unconscious person, then make-up is necessarily obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm with dalil) from the Hanafi school regarding a person who remains unconscious (mughma 'alayh) for the entire month of Ramadan. The ruling is that such a person must make up (qada) all the missed fasts. The Hanafi reasoning distinguishes unconsciousness from insanity: unconsciousness veils the intellect but does not remove it, similar to sleep, while insanity removes it entirely. The proof includes the Quranic verse (68:2) negating madness from the Prophet despite his unconsciousness. Hasan al-Basri's view (no make-up) is rejected because he considered unconsciousness as removing addressability, but the Hanafi position holds that the obligation of the month (sabab) is present, and the person remains a legal subject (mukhatab). Therefore, make-up is fard.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighma'): Unconsciousness; a state where intellect is veiled but not removed, considered a valid excuse for delaying worship but not for dropping it entirely.
جنون (junun): Insanity; a state where intellect is completely destroyed, removing legal addressability and obligations.
حرج (haraj): Hardship or difficulty that warrants concession in Islamic law; excuses that cause continuous hardship can exempt from obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who remains unconscious (mughma 'alayh) for the entire month of Ramadan?
A) No make-up (qada) is required.
B) Make-up (qada) is required.
C) Make-up is required only if unconsciousness exceeds 15 days.
D) It is optional.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, unconsciousness for the entire Ramadan does not exempt from qada; the fasts must be made up because the intellect remains, and the obligation is merely delayed.

Classification of Valid Excuses (A'dhar) for Fasting and Their Rulings
Definition and classification of valid excuses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of valid excuses ('adhar) for fasting according to the Hanafi school and state the ruling for each type.

Core Idea
Excuses are classified by duration and hardship; continuous total hardship drops all obligations.

The Hanafi school classifies valid excuses ('adhar) that affect fasting obligations based on their usual duration and the degree of hardship (haraj) they cause. Understanding this classification is essential for determining when a missed fast must be made up (qada) and when the obligation is totally waived. In this passage from Kafayah, the author delineates four categories with distinct rulings derived from the overarching principle of removing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying principle
The passage uses 'haraj' (hardship) as the basis for exemptions. Excuses causing continuous hardship over the entire worship period lead to dropping the obligation entirely.
This principle is derived from the Quranic maxim 'la haraj' (no hardship).
2
List the four types of excuses
The four types are: sleep (nawm), minority (adam al-bulugh), unconsciousness (ighma'), and insanity (junun). Note their characteristic durations.
These are the only categories mentioned in the text; other excuses may be analogized.
3
Apply the 'mumtad' criteria
Determine whether each excuse normally extends for one day and night (for fasting) and/or for one prayer time (for salah). The ruling depends on this.
Mumtad means 'extending continuously'; the text uses 'mumtad' implicitly.
4
Derive the ruling for each type
Sleep: no exemption. Minority: all worship dropped. Unconsciousness: if exceeds one day and night, qada of salah (up to six prayers) is dropped, but fasting qada is not exempted. Insanity: if it extends for the entire duration of Ramadan, both salah and fasting are dropped.
The text explicitly mentions only the salah qada exemption for unconsciousness; fasting qada remains mandatory.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a person is insane for the entire Ramadan, no qada is required for either salah or fasting. This is because the excuse is mamdud (extensive) for both.
The text concludes with this example to illustrate the fourth type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب کفایہ لکھتے ہیں کہ اعذار چار قسم کے ہوتے ہیں، ایک وہ جو عادہ ایک دن رات تک ممتد نہ ہوتا ہو جیسے نوم اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس کی وجہ سے کوئی عبادت ساقہ نہیں ہوتی کیونکہ یہ عذر موجب حرج نہیں ہوتا ۔ ۲) دوسرے وہ عذر جو پیدائش اور خلقی طور پر متد ہوتا ہے جیسے عدم بلوغ کا زمانہ اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ یہ تمام عبادت کو ساقط کر دیتا ہے کیونکہ یہ موجب حرج ہے پس حرج دور کرنے کے لئے بچہ تمام عبادتیں ساقط ہو گئیں۔ (۳) تیسرے وہ عذر جو عادۃ ایک نماز کے وقت کی مقدار تو ممتد ہوتا ہے لیکن روزے کے وقت کی مقدار ممتد نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسے اغماء اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک دن رات سے زائد ہو گیا رفع حرج کے لئے اس کو عذر قرار دیا جائے گا۔ یعنی اگر اغماء کی وجہ سے چھ نمازیں فوت ہو جائیں تو ان کی قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی یعنی حرج دور کرنے کے لئے قضاء ساقط ہوگئی۔ (۴) چہارم یہ کہ وقت نماز اور وقت صوم دونوں کو ممتد ہو جاتا ہے اور کبھی ممتد نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسے جنون اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر دونوں میں ممتد ہو گیا تو دونوں کو ساقط کر دے گا۔ اس تفصیل کے بعد واضح ہو کہ اگر کوئی شخص رمضان بھر مجنون رہا تو اس پر اس رمضان کے روزوں کی قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی ۔
The author of Kafayah writes that excuses are of four types: (1) That which usually does not extend for a day and night, like sleep. Its ruling is that no worship is dropped because this excuse does not cause hardship. (2) Second, the excuse that is congenital and created, like the time of non-bulugh (minority). Its ruling is that it drops all worship because it causes hardship. So to remove hardship, the child's worship is all dropped. (3) Third, the excuse that usually extends for the time of one prayer but does not extend for the time of fasting, like unconsciousness. Its ruling is that if it exceeds one day and night, then to remove hardship, it is considered an excuse. That is, if six prayers are missed due to unconsciousness, their make-up is not required; i.e., make-up is dropped to remove hardship. (4) Fourth, that which extends for the time of both prayer and fasting but sometimes does not extend, like insanity. Its ruling is that if it extends in both, it drops both. After this detail, it is clear that if a person remains insane for the entire Ramadan, then make-up of that Ramadan's fasts is not obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification (taqsim) of valid excuses ('adhar) that affect the obligation of fasting. The author of Kafayah (likely a Hanafi scholar) categorizes excuses into four types based on their usual duration and the extent of hardship they cause. The principle applied is 'haraj' (hardship): if an excuse causes continuous hardship throughout the period of worship, the obligation is dropped entirely; if not, then only a concession is given. The four types are: sleep (non-extensive, no exemption), minority (extensive due to nature, total exemption), unconsciousness (extends for salah time but not sawm, exemption only for salah qada), and insanity (may extend for both, thus exemption for both when it does). The passage concludes by applying this to the case of a full month of insanity, where no make-up is required.

Key Terms
اعذار (a'dhar): Plural of 'udhr; legal excuses that allow concessions or exemptions in worship obligations.
حرج (haraj): Hardship or difficulty that is continuous and significant, used to justify concessions (rukhsa).
ممتدد (mumtad): Extending continuously; used to describe an excuse that lasts the entire duration of a worship period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Kafayah classification, what type of excuse is minority (non-bulugh)?
A) Non-extensive, no exemption
B) Congenital and extensive, total exemption
C) Extensive for salah only
D) Extensive for both salah and sawm

Key Takeaway
Valid excuses in Hanafi law are classified by their usual duration and concomitant hardship, determining whether worship obligations are partially or totally dropped.

When Insanity Excuses Fasting: Hanafi vs. Maliki
Hanafi ruling that insanity for entire month exempts fasting, opposed by Imam Malik

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between insanity and unconsciousness for fasting qada, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
Continuous hardship (haraj) exempts; rare hardship does not.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position that a person who remains insane for the entire month of Ramadan is not required to make up the fasts, contrasting with Imam Malik who analogizes insanity to unconsciousness. The key difference lies in the typical duration of each condition and the resulting hardship.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding a person insane for the full month of Ramadan regarding qada?
No qada required.
2
Identify the opposing view
What is Imam Malik's position and his basis?
He holds qada is required, analogizing to unconsciousness.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
How does Imam Malik justify his analogy? What is the 'illa (effective cause)?
Both insanity and unconsciousness involve corruption of intellect.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Why does the Hanafi school reject this analogy? What principle is used?
The principle of hardship (haraj): insanity typically lasts the whole month, causing hardship, while unconsciousness rarely does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام مالک فرماتے ہیں کہ جنون کی صورت میں بھی قضاء واجب ہو جائے گی ۔ حضرت امام مالک نے جنون کو اغماء پر قیاس کیا ہے کیونکہ اغماء کی طرح جنون بھی فساد عقل پیدا کرتا ہے پس جس طرح انماء کی صورت میں قضاء واجب ہوتی ہے اسی طرح جنون کی صورت میں بھی قضاء واجب ہوگی۔
ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ سابقہ اقسام اربعہ سے معلوم ہو گیا کہ روزہ یا نماز کو ساقط کرنے والی چیز حرج ہے یعنی اگر عذر ایسا ہو جو موجب حرج ہے تو عبادت ذمہ سے ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر موجب حرج نہ ہو تو اس عذر کی وجہ سے عبادت ساقط نہ ہو گی ۔ اب ہم نے دیکھا کہ انتماء بالعموم ایک ماہ تک باقی نہیں رہتا۔ اب اگر کبھی ایک ماہ تک اعفاء ممتد ہو گیا تو چونکہ یہ ایک نادر چیز ہے اس لئے ایک ماہ کے روزوں کی قضاء کرنے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہوگا ۔ اور جنون چونکہ عادہ ایک ماہ کا استیعاب کر لیتا ہے اس لئے جنون کی صورت میں ایک ماہ کے روزوں کی قضاء کرنے میں حرج لازم آئے گا۔ پس جب یہ بات واضح ہوگی کہ اعماء کی صورت میں حرج نہیں اور جنون کی صورت میں حرج ہے تو ہم نے کہا کہ پورے ماہ رمضان مجنون رہنے کی صورت میں قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی لیکن پورے ماہ رمضان اغماء کی صورت میں قضاء واجب ہوگی۔
Imam Malik says that in the case of insanity as well, make-up will be obligatory. Imam Malik analogized insanity to unconsciousness because insanity also causes corruption of intellect; so just as make-up is obligatory in case of unconsciousness, similarly in case of insanity make-up will be obligatory. Our proof is that the previous classification of four types has shown that the thing that drops fasting or prayer is hardship. That is, if the excuse is such that it causes hardship, then the worship will be dropped from the liability. And if it does not cause hardship, then the worship will not be dropped due to that excuse. Now we see that unconsciousness generally does not remain for a whole month. If it ever extends to a month, then because it is a rare thing, there is no hardship in making up fasts of one month. And since insanity usually covers the whole month, therefore in the case of insanity, making up fasts of one month would entail hardship. So when it is clear that in unconsciousness there is no hardship, and in insanity there is hardship, we say that in the case of remaining insane for the whole month of Ramadan, make-up is not obligatory, but in the case of remaining unconscious for the whole month of Ramadan, make-up is obligatory.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding the obligation of make-up (qada) for a person who remains insane (majnun) for the entire month of Ramadan. Imam Malik analogizes insanity to unconsciousness, arguing that both involve corruption of intellect, so make-up should be required in both. The Hanafi response relies on the principle of hardship (haraj) established in the previous classification: unconsciousness rarely lasts a full month, so requiring make-up does not cause hardship, whereas insanity typically does, so exemption is granted to remove hardship. Thus, no make-up is required for insanity, but it is required for unconsciousness.

Key Terms
جنون (junun): Insanity; a state where intellect is completely destroyed, continuously present for extended periods, causing hardship.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used by Imam Malik to equate insanity with unconsciousness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school hold that a person insane for the entire month of Ramadan does not have to make up the fasts?
A) Because insanity removes intellect completely, so the person is not obligated at all.
B) Because of continuous hardship (haraj) that would result if make-up were required.
C) Because insanity is always a voluntary condition.
D) Because the Quran explicitly exempts the insane.

Key Takeaway
Insanity for the entire month exempts from qada because of continuous hardship; unconsciousness does not, as it rarely lasts that long.

No Qada for Insanity All Ramadan: The Hanafi-Maliki Dispute
Debate on qada for one insane all Ramadan: Hanafi vs Maliki

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for a person insane the entire Ramadan, explain the Hanafi proof based on haraj, and evaluate the Maliki counterargument.

Core Idea
If an excuse typically covers the entire month, requiring qada causes hardship (haraj) and the obligation drops.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding a person who remained insane for the entire month of Ramadan. The Hanafi school holds that no qada is required, while Imam Malik argues for qada by analogy to unconsciousness. The key is how each school evaluates 'hardship' (haraj) in the context of prolonged excuses. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle of haraj in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the ruling on qada for a person insane all Ramadan according to the Hanafi school?
The passage states: 'qada is not wajib'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school disagrees and what do they hold?
Imam Malik holds qada is wajib.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does Imam Malik present? Trace the qiyas.
He analogizes insanity to unconsciousness because both corrupt intellect.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut this qiyas?
By distinguishing based on typical duration and the concept of haraj.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in ruling between the two schools?
A Hanafi does not make up fasts; a Maliki must.

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد سوم
۲۵۱
كتاب الصوم
(۴) چہارم یہ کہ وقت نماز اور وقت صوم دونوں کو ممتد ہو جاتا ہے اور کبھی ممتد نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسے جنون اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر دونوں میں ممتد ہو گیا تو دونوں کو ساقط کر دے گا۔ اس تفصیل کے بعد واضح ہو کہ اگر کوئی شخص رمضان بھر مجنون رہا تو اس پر اس رمضان کے روزوں کی قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی ۔ امام مالک فرماتے ہیں کہ جنون کی صورت میں بھی قضاء واجب ہو جائے گی ۔ حضرت امام مالک نے جنون کو اغماء پر قیاس کیا ہے کیونکہ اغماء کی طرح جنون بھی فساد عقل پیدا کرتا ہے پس جس طرح انماء کی صورت میں قضاء واجب ہوتی ہے اسی طرح جنون کی صورت میں بھی قضاء واجب ہوگی۔
ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ سابقہ اقسام اربعہ سے معلوم ہو گیا کہ روزہ یا نماز کو ساقط کرنے والی چیز حرج ہے یعنی اگر عذر ایسا ہو جو موجب حرج ہے تو عبادت ذمہ سے ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر موجب حرج نہ ہو تو اس عذر کی وجہ سے عبادت ساقط نہ ہو گی ۔ اب ہم نے دیکھا کہ انتماء بالعموم ایک ماہ تک باقی نہیں رہتا۔ اب اگر کبھی ایک ماہ تک اعفاء ممتد ہو گیا تو چونکہ یہ ایک نادر چیز ہے اس لئے ایک ماہ کے روزوں کی قضاء کرنے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہوگا ۔ اور جنون چونکہ عادہ ایک ماہ کا استیعاب کر لیتا ہے اس لئے جنون کی صورت میں ایک ماہ کے روزوں کی قضاء کرنے میں حرج لازم آئے گا۔ پس جب یہ بات واضح ہوگی کہ اعماء کی صورت میں حرج نہیں اور جنون کی صورت میں حرج ہے تو ہم نے کہا کہ پورے ماہ رمضان مجنون رہنے کی صورت میں قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی لیکن پورے ماہ رمضان اغماء کی صورت میں قضاء واجب ہوگی۔
The explanation of Hidaya, volume three, page 251, Book of Fasting. (4) Fourth: that the time of prayer and the time of fasting both can become prolonged and sometimes not prolonged. Like insanity: its ruling is that if it becomes prolonged in both, it will cause both to be dropped. After this detail, it becomes clear that if a person remained insane throughout Ramadan, then the qada of the fasts of that Ramadan will not be obligatory upon him. Imam Malik says that in the case of insanity, qada becomes obligatory. Imam Malik analogized insanity to unconsciousness because insanity, like unconsciousness, causes corruption of intellect. So just as qada is obligatory in the case of unconsciousness, similarly qada will be obligatory in the case of insanity. Our proof is that from the previous four categories it became known that the thing that drops fasting or prayer is hardship (haraj). That is, if the excuse is such that it causes hardship, then the worship is dropped from the liability. And if it does not cause hardship, then the worship is not dropped due to that excuse. Now we see that unconsciousness generally does not remain for a whole month. If it ever becomes prolonged for a month, since it is a rare thing, there is no hardship in making up the fasts of one month. And because insanity customarily covers a month, making up the fasts of one month in the case of insanity necessarily involves hardship. So when it becomes clear that in the case of unconsciousness there is no hardship and in the case of insanity there is hardship, we said that if one remains insane the whole month of Ramadan, qada is not obligatory, but if one remains unconscious the whole month of Ramadan, qada is obligatory.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding whether a person who is insane for the entire month of Ramadan must make up the missed fasts. The Hanafi position (as stated by the author of al-Hidayah and its commentator) is that no qada is required. The key reasoning is based on the concept of haraj (hardship). The Hanafi school classifies excuses that drop obligations into categories. Here, the specific criterion is whether the excuse (here, insanity) causes overwhelming hardship if qada were required. Since insanity commonly lasts a full month (and thus making up a whole month's fasts would be burdensome), qada is not wajib. In contrast, unconsciousness (ighma') rarely lasts a full month, so requiring qada for a rare occurrence does not constitute hardship. Imam Malik analogizes insanity to unconsciousness, arguing that both are states of mental incapacity, so they should have the same ruling. The Hanafi response distinguishes based on the typical duration: because insanity typically covers a full month, requiring qada would cause haraj, whereas unconsciousness does not. Thus the Hanafi ruling is specific to the factual pattern of full-month insanity.

Key Terms
حرج (haraj): Hardship or difficulty that is considered legally significant to lift an obligation.
قضاء (qada): Making up an obligatory act after its prescribed time has passed.
اغماء (ighma'): Unconsciousness or coma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is qada not required for a person insane for the entire Ramadan?
A) Because insanity completely removes legal responsibility (ahliyyah).
B) Because requiring qada would cause overwhelming hardship (haraj) due to the typical duration of insanity.
C) Because the Quranic verse only mentions witnessing the month, not insanity.
D) Because the Prophet ﷺ exempted the insane from all obligations.

Key Takeaway
When an excuse (like insanity) typically lasts the entire duration of an obligatory act, requiring qada would cause haraj, so the obligation is dropped—this is the Hanafi ruling.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
12 min
Partial Insanity and Qada: Hanafi Position with Proof
Ruling on qada for one insane part of Ramadan with dispute and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for partial insanity during Ramadan, identify the opposing views of Zufar and Shafi'i, and explain the Hanafi proof using Quran 2:185 and the concepts of dhimma and faydah.

Core Idea
Partial insanity during Ramadan triggers qada upon recovery; obligation is anchored in dhimma, not performance capacity.

This lesson examines a well-known dispute: if a person is insane for part of Ramadan and then regains sanity, must he make up the missed fasts? The Hanafi school, as laid out in al-Hidayah, holds that qada is wajib. Imam Zufar (within the Hanafi school) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree. We will explore the Quranic proof, the concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah), and the role of benefit (faydah) in determining the obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: qada of missed fasts is wajib for the person who regains sanity during Ramadan.
The ruling applies to partial insanity, not full-month insanity.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that qada is not wajib because the person lacked eligibility (ahliyyah) during insanity.
They analogize to full-month insanity where qada is dropped due to absence of performance capacity.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof from Quran
The verse 'Whoever witnesses the month must fast it' (2:185) is interpreted to mean that witnessing part of the month obligates fasting the entire month. The pronoun in 'falyasumhu' refers to the full month, so missed days must be made up.
The Hanafi tafsir uses taqdir (implied meaning) of 'ba'd al-shahr' for 'witnessing the month'.
4
Understand the Hanafi response on eligibility
Eligibility is of two kinds: ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for liability) and ahliyyat al-ada (capacity for performance). The insane have the first via dhimma, so the obligation attaches; qada becomes wajib because performance was impossible, not because it was obligatory at the time.
For full-month insanity, qada is not wajib due to lack of faydah (benefit) – hardship in making up a full month.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi holds qada wajib; Shafi'i holds no qada. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the Zahir al-Riwayah position (qada wajib, no distinction between original and acquired insanity).
Imam Muhammad's distinction (original vs acquired) is a narration but not the relied-upon view.
