If the insane person becomes sane during (the month), he makes up what passed, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. They say: performance (ada) was not obligatory upon him due to lack of eligibility (ahliyyah), and qada is dependent on it, so it is like the one who covered the whole month. Our proof: the cause (sabab) existed, which is the month, and eligibility is by virtue of being a responsible person (dhimma), and in obligation there is benefit (faydah), namely that he becomes required in a way that is not burdensome in performance, unlike the one who covered the whole month, because he would experience hardship in performance, so there is no benefit. Its completion is in al-Khilafiyyat. Then there is no difference between original and acquired (insanity) before this in Zahir al-Riwayah, and from Muhammad it is narrated that he distinguished between them, because if he reaches puberty insane, he joins the child, so address (khitab) is absent, unlike if he reaches puberty sane then becomes insane, and this is the choice of some later scholars. Translation: And if the insane person becomes sane during some part of Ramadan, he makes up the past days. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i differ: both say performance was not obligatory on him due to absence of eligibility, and qada is dependent upon it, so it is like one who was insane the whole month. And our proof: the cause, i.e., witnessing the month, exists, and eligibility is by dhimma, and in making it obligatory there is benefit, namely that he is required in such a way that there is no hardship in performing it. Unlike the one covered (the whole month) because he will feel hardship in performance, so there is no benefit. And its complete discussion is in al-Khilafiyyat. Then there is no difference between original and acquired insanity before this in Zahir al-Riwayah, and from Muhammad it is narrated that he distinguished between them because if he reaches puberty insane, he joins the child, so the address is absent, unlike if he reaches puberty sane then becomes insane, and this is the choice of some later scholars. Explanation of the case: If the insane person becomes sane during some part of Ramadan, he makes up the past days and fasts the remaining days. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i say that qada of past days is not wajib upon him. This is also the saying of Imam Ahmad. Their proof: the insane person did not have the capacity for address, so performance was not wajib on him, and qada is dependent on performance, so when performance was not wajib on him for the days before regaining sanity, qada is also not wajib. And it is like one who remained insane the whole month: just as qada is not wajib for the whole month, similarly qada is not wajib if he is insane for some days. They analogized partial insanity to full-month insanity. Our proof: The person who was insane for some days of Ramadan and then regained sanity, the cause for fasting being wajib upon him is witnessing the month, because Allah says: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' And it is settled that 'month' in the verse means part of the month, because if the whole month were the cause, then the fast would have to be observed in Shawwal, since the effect comes after the cause. So the implied expression is: 'Whoever among you witnesses part of the month, let him fast the entire month.' In this verse, the pronoun in 'falyasumhu' refers back to the word 'al-shahr' mentioned in the text, not 'ba'd al-shahr' which is assumed. So the meaning is: The person whose insanity did not cover the entire month of Ramadan has witnessed part of the month, so he must fast the entire month. But because he could not fast some days due to insanity, he makes them up and fasts the rest. 'And eligibility is by dhimma' is the answer to a question. The question is: For the insane person, although the cause (witnessing the month) exists, there is a preventing factor for making the fasts of the earlier days wajib upon him, namely the absence of eligibility during the time of insanity. And it is obvious that for the obligation of fasting, besides the cause, the capacity of the one being addressed (ahliyyat al-mukallaf) is necessary. That is why fasting Ramadan is not wajib upon a minor, although the cause (witnessing the month) exists, but since eligibility is absent, Ramadan fasting is not wajib upon him. Similarly, the person who was insane during that time: eligibility for obligation was not present. And when eligibility was not present, performance for that time could not be wajib, and when performance was not wajib, qada should also not be wajib, yet you make qada wajib for past days. The answer: The basis of eligibility is only that this person be capable of responsibility and liability; that is, although the insane person does not actually have the ability to fast, he certainly has the capacity that fasting can be made a liability upon him, and the basis of taklif is this eligibility. So when this eligibility exists, the fasts of the period of insanity become obligatory upon him. But since he could not perform them, qada becomes wajib. But if it is asked: if this is correct, then for the person who was insane the whole Ramadan, qada should also be wajib, yet you do not make qada wajib. The answer: Mere capacity to accept liability is not sufficient; there must also be benefit (faydah). And the benefit is that fasting be required in such a way that there is no hardship in performing it. So we see that in making less than a month's fasts obligatory, there is benefit because... (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a person who is insane for part of Ramadan and then regains sanity before the month ends. The Hanafi position, stated by the author of al-Hidayah, is that such a person must make up (qada) the fasts he missed due to insanity and fast the remaining days. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that qada is not wajib because the person lacked eligibility (ahliyyah) during the period of insanity. The Hanafi response relies on the Quranic verse 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185). The interpretation is that 'witnessing the month' means witnessing part of it, so the cause (sabab) of obligation is partial presence in Ramadan. Eligibility (ahliyyah) is understood as being a responsible person (dhimma) capable of bearing liability, not the actual ability to perform the act. Therefore, the obligation attaches to the person's liability even during insanity, but because actual performance was impossible, qada becomes required after regaining sanity. The passage also discusses a nuance: for a person insane the entire month, qada is not wajib because there is no benefit (faydah)—making up a full month's fasts would cause hardship. For partial insanity, making up less than a month is feasible without excessive hardship, so qada is beneficial. Finally, the text mentions a distinction between original insanity (from birth or before puberty) and acquired insanity (after puberty). In Zahir al-Riwayah, no distinction is made. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani is reported to distinguish: one who reaches puberty already insane is like a child (no address), whereas one who becomes sane then insane remains addressed. Some later scholars prefer this distinction.

Key Terms
اہلیت (ahliyyah): Legal capacity. Here, the text distinguishes between capacity for liability (ahliyyat al-wujub, by dhimma) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada). The insane person has the former but not the latter.
ذمہ (dhimma): The legal personality or liability of a person; the foundation of binding obligations.
فائدہ (faydah): Benefit or practical utility in making an obligation; if no benefit (e.g., due to hardship), the obligation may be dropped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah), what is the ruling on qada for a person who was insane for the first 10 days of Ramadan and then regained sanity?
A) He must make up the 10 missed fasts (qada).
B) He does not have to make up any fasts because he witnessed part of the month.
C) He must fast the entire month from the day he regained sanity, and no qada.
D) He may choose to make up or not.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires qada for fasts missed due to partial insanity during Ramadan, based on the Quranic imperative and the concepts of dhimma and faydah.

Sawm — Fasting
advanced
12 min
Qada' for a Mad Person Who Recovers During Ramadan: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Differences
Explains obligation of qada' for partially insane person with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada' for a person who recovers from insanity during Ramadan, explain the proofs from Quran and benefit/hardship analysis, and distinguish between congenital and acquired insanity per Imam Muhammad's preferred view.

Core Idea
Qada' is wajib for missed fasts if insanity lasted less than a month; dhimmah capacity suffices for obligation.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a nuanced issue: what happens when a person who is insane for part of Ramadan regains consciousness? The author presents the majority Hanafi position that qada' is required, then explores an intra-school disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the Zahir al-Riwayah regarding congenital versus acquired insanity. The underlying legal principles involve the capacity of the dhimmah, the cause of obligation (witnessing the month), and the role of hardship in waiving obligations.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the central ruling
Determine the basic Hanafi position: a person who recovers from insanity during Ramadan must make up (qada') the missed fasts for the days he was insane, provided the insanity lasted less than a month.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and the majority of the Hanafi school.
2
2. Analyze the opposing argument
Examine the argument of Imam Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad: they hold no qada' because the insane lack ahliyyat al-khitab, so the original obligation (ada) never existed, and qada' cannot arise. They analogize to full-month insanity.
Their proof is based on qiyas: partial month insanity analogous to full month.
3
3. Understand the Hanafi dalil
Study the Hanafi proof from Quran 2:185: the one who witnesses any part of the month must fast the entire month. The verse is interpreted such that 'witnessing the month' includes witnessing part of it. The obligation is placed on the dhimmah, which a mad person still possesses.
The imperative 'falyasumhu' indicates wujub; the pronoun refers to the entire month.
4
4. Grasp the benefit/hardship limitation
Note that the Hanafi response limits qada' to cases where the insanity is less than a month. For a full month or more, qada' is dropped due to hardship (haraj). This is based on the principle that obligations that cause excessive hardship are waived.
This distinguishes insanity from childhood: childhood is always prolonged, so qada' is never required; insanity can be short or long.
5
5. Differentiate congenital vs. acquired insanity
Learn Imam Muhammad's distinction: congenital insanity (before puberty) is like childhood – if recovery occurs during Ramadan, no qada' for past days. Acquired insanity (after puberty) requires qada' if less than a month. Some later scholars prefer this view.
Zahir al-Riwayah treats both types equally; Imam Muhammad's view is based on analogy to a child becoming baligh.

کا جواب یہ ہے کہ خالی ذمہ داری قبول کرنے کے لائق ہونا کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ اس میں فائدہ بھی ہو۔ اور فائدہ یہ ہے کہ ایسے طریقہ پر روزہ رکھنا مطلوب ہو کہ اس کو ادا کرنے میں کوئی حرج لاحق نہ ہو۔ پس ہم نہیں ہوتا اس لئے قضاء کو ساقط نہیں کرتا اور عدم بلوغ یعنی بچپنہ چونکہ طویل ہوتا ہے اس لئے وہ قضاء کو بالکلیہ ساقط کر دیتا ہے۔ اور جنون طویل بھی ہوتا ہے اور مختصر بھی ہوتا ہے لہذا اگر طویل ہوا تو وہ عدم بلوغ کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گا اور اگر طویل نہ ہوا تو اغماء کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گا۔ اور روزہ کے اندر جنون کی مقدار طویل ایک ماہ ہے اور نماز کے اندر ایک دن رات سے زائد ہے پس رمضان کا پورا مہینہ اگر جنون کی حالت میں گزرا تو قضاء ساقط ہو جائے گی ۔ اور اگر اس سے کم مجنون رہا تو قضاء ساقط نہ ہو گی۔ اس مسئلہ کی پوری تفصیل خلافیات میں مذکور ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جنون اصلی اور جنون عارضی کے درمیان کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔ یعنی سابقہ حکم میں دونوں برابر ہیں۔ بعض حضرات کہتے ہیں کہ یہ عدم فرق ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق ہے۔ اور امام محمد نے دونوں کے درمیان فرق کیا ہے چنانچہ انہوں نے فرمایا ہے کہ جنون اصلی کی صورت میں اگر رمضان کے کسی حصہ میں افاقہ ہو گیا تو اس پر ایام گذشتہ کی قضاء واجب نہیں ہوگی ۔ جیسا کہ بچہ اگر درمیان رمضان بالغ ہو گیا تو اس پر ایام گذشتہ کی قضاء واجب نہیں ہوتی اور جنون عارضی کی صورت میں ایام گذشتہ کی قضا واجب ہو جائے گی۔ حضرت امام محمد کا بیان کردہ فرق بعض متاخرین کا پسندیدہ مذہب ہے فوائد جنون اصلی یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص بالغ ہونے سے پہلے جنون عارضی یہ ہے کہ بالغ تو عاقل ہو کر ہوا مگر بعد میں مجنون ہو گیا ۔ مجنون تھا پھر بالغ بھی جنون ہی کی حالت میں ہوا۔ اور
When a mad person attained puberty, he became attached to the child, so the address was nullified, contrary to when he attained puberty while sane then became mad, and this is preferred by some later scholars. Explanation of the case: If a mad person regained consciousness during some part of Ramadan, then he should make up the past days and keep the upcoming fasts. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i say that making up the past days is not obligatory upon him. This is also the saying of Imam Ahmad. Their proof is that since the mad person did not possess the capacity for address (ahliyyat al-khitab), the original performance (ada) was not obligatory upon him, and since qada' is dependent on ada, when ada was not obligatory before regaining consciousness, qada' is also not obligatory. This is like someone who remained mad for the entire month of Ramadan; just as in that case qada' is not obligatory, similarly if he was mad for some days of Ramadan, qada' is not obligatory. They analogized the occurrence of insanity during part of Ramadan to the case of remaining insane for the whole month. Our proof is that a person who remained mad for some days of Ramadan and then regained consciousness, the cause for fasting being obligatory upon him is witnessing the month; because Allah Ta'ala says: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' And it is settled that in the verse, 'the month' refers to part of the month, because if the entire month were made the cause, then the fast would have to be kept in Shawwal. Since the effect (mujab) occurs after the cause. So the implied phrase would be: 'So whoever among you witnesses part of the month, let him fast the entire month.' In this verse, the pronoun in 'falyasumhu' refers to the word 'shahr' which is mentioned in the phrase, not 'ba'd al-shahr' which is considered implied. The meaning is: Whoever's madness did not encompass the entire month of Ramadan, he has witnessed part of the month, so he should fast the entire month, but because due to madness he could not fast some days, he should make them up and perform the rest as ada. The answer to the question is based on 'al-ahliyyah bi al-dhimmah'. The question is: Although the cause (shuhud al-shahr) existed for the mad person, there is a preventer (mani') for making the fasts of the earlier days obligatory upon him, i.e., the absence of capacity (ahliyyah) during the time of madness. It is obvious that for the obligation of fasting, besides the cause, the capacity of the mukallaf is also necessary, which is why Ramadan fasting is not obligatory upon a minor, even though the cause (shuhud al-shahr) is present, but because capacity is non-existent, Ramadan fasting is not obligatory upon him. Similarly, a person who was mad at a certain time did not possess the capacity for obligation at that time, so the original performance for that period was not obligatory, and when original performance was not obligatory, qada' should also not be obligatory, yet you (the Hanafis) make qada' for the past days obligatory. The answer is: The criterion for capacity is only that the person be worthy of responsibility (dhimmah) and charge. So although a mad person actually does not have the capacity to fast, he certainly has the capacity that fasting can be placed as a duty upon him, and the basis of taklif is this capacity. So when such capacity is present, the fasts of the period of madness also become incumbent upon him, but since he could not perform them, qada' is obligatory upon him. But if it is asked: If this is correct, then a person who remained mad for the entire Ramadan should also have qada' obligatory, yet you do not make qada' obligatory upon him. The answer is: Mere capacity to accept responsibility is not sufficient; there must also be benefit. The benefit is that fasting be required in such a way that its performance does not involve hardship. So we see that there is benefit in making fasts less than a month obligatory, because making up less than a month involves no hardship. Therefore, when fasts less than a month are made obligatory, qada' becomes obligatory. But in the case of a month or more spent in madness, there is no benefit in making fasts obligatory, because qada' of a month's fasts is dropped due to hardship. So if a month's fasts were made obligatory, they would be dropped due to hardship, so there is no benefit in making qada' obligatory in that case. The summary is: Obligation upon the dhimmah (wujub fi al-dhimmah) is not nullified due to non-bulugh or insanity, but because coma (ighma) is not prolonged, it does not drop qada', and non-bulugh (childhood) is prolonged, so it drops qada' completely. Insanity can be both prolonged and brief; if prolonged, it joins non-bulugh; if brief, it joins coma. For fasting, the measure of prolonged insanity is one month; for prayer, it is more than a day and night. So if the entire month of Ramadan passes in insanity, qada' is dropped. If less than that, qada' is not dropped. Full detail of this issue is mentioned in the disputations. The author of Hidayah says: There is no difference between congenital insanity ('asli) and acquired insanity ('aradi); they are equal in the previous ruling. Some say this lack of difference is according to Zahir al-Riwayah. Imam Muhammad differentiated between them: He said that in the case of congenital insanity, if he regains consciousness during any part of Ramadan, qada' of the past days is not obligatory upon him, just as when a child becomes baligh during Ramadan, qada' of the past days is not obligatory. In the case of acquired insanity, qada' of the past days becomes obligatory. The difference stated by Imam Muhammad is the preferred madhhab of some later scholars. Benefits: Congenital insanity means that a person was insane before attaining puberty. Acquired insanity means that a person attained puberty while sane but later became insane. He was insane and then attained puberty while still in a state of insanity. And
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the ruling on qada' (making up missed fasts) for a person who is insane during part of Ramadan and then recovers. The author begins by stating a preferred position of some later scholars regarding the difference between congenital and acquired insanity. Then he presents the case: if a mad person comes to his senses during Ramadan, he must make up the days he missed and fast the remaining days. Imam Zufar, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad hold that no qada' is required for the past days. Their reasoning is that the insane person lacks the capacity for address (ahliyyat al-khitab), so the original obligation (ada) never applied, and qada' follows ada. They analogize partial insanity to full-month insanity, where all agree no qada' is due. The Hanafi response (based on Abu Hanifa's position) cites Quran 2:185, arguing that 'witnessing the month' means witnessing any part of it; thus the entire month becomes obligatory. The insane person's dhimmah (legal capacity) is sufficient for obligation even if actual performance is impossible. However, the author addresses the objection: if dhimmah suffices, then why is qada' not required for the entire month of insanity? The answer is based on benefit and hardship: for less than a month, no hardship; for a full month or more, qada' is dropped due to hardship (haraj). The passage then compares insanity to other states like childhood and coma, concluding that prolonged insanity (a month for fasting) nullifies qada', while shorter insanity does not. Finally, the author notes that the Hidayah states no difference between congenital and acquired insanity, but Imam Muhammad differentiated. The 'benefits' gives concise definitions: congenital insanity is before puberty, acquired is after.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed obligatory act after its designated time has passed, specifically here the missed fasts of Ramadan.
ادا (ada): Performing an obligatory act within its prescribed time.
اہلیت (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to receive obligations; the text distinguishes between capacity for actual performance (failed during insanity) and capacity for being held liable (dhimmah remains).
جنون اصلی (junun asli): Congenital insanity existing before puberty.
جنون عارضی (junun 'aradi): Acquired insanity after puberty while sane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's preferred view, what is the ruling on qada' for a person who had congenital insanity (junun asli) and recovered during Ramadan?
A) Qada' is obligatory for the missed days.
B) Qada' is not obligatory for the missed days.
C) Qada' is obligatory only if the insanity lasted more than a month.
D) Qada' is obligatory but must be done with tawbah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires qada' for missed fasts if insanity lasted less than a month, but Imam Muhammad's distinction (congenital vs. acquired insanity) is preferred by later scholars, waiving qada' for congenital cases.

Fasts Without Intention: Ruling for One Who Neither Intends Fast Nor Break Fast
Presents ruling and dispute on validity of fast without niyyah for healthy resident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a fast without intention for a healthy resident and evaluate Zufar's counter-argument using the analogy of zakat.

Core Idea
Fasting requires intention; mere abstention is not worship.

This passage tackles a subtle yet common scenario: a healthy person in Ramadan who, without intending to fast or break it, simply refrains from eating and drinking. Does this count as a valid fast? The Hanafi school says no, requiring intention for all worship. But a fellow Hanafi jurist, Imam Zufar, dissents. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of the role of intention (niyyah) in 'ibadah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi and Zufar's positions
First, note the two conflicting rulings: the Hanafi school requires qada' (makeup) for a day without intention; Zufar considers it valid for a healthy resident. State each position concisely.
Zufar's view is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
Analyze Zufar's proof
Zufar argues that imsak (abstention) is legally obligatory (mustahaqq) by virtue of Ramadan, so any abstention—even without intention—fulfills the obligation. He analogizes to giving away one's entire nisab to the poor without explicit intention of zakat.
The analogy assumes that zakat is discharged without niyyah, which the Hanafi school disputes.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal emphasizes that the imsak required in Ramadan is specifically an act of worship ('ibadah), and all 'ibadah requires niyyah. In the zakat analogy, the act of giving inherently implies closeness to Allah (qurbah), so intention is implicit—unlike mere abstention.
This distinction is crucial: the nature of the act determines whether intention is explicit or implicit.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: a person who refrains from eating all day without intention—according to Zufar, his fast counts; according to the Hanafis, he must make up that day. This affects the validity of his Ramadan.
The tarjih (preferred view) is the Hanafi position, as it is the relied-upon fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ لَمْ يَنْوِفِي رَمَضَانَ كُلَّهَ لَا صَوْمًا وَلَا فِطْرًا فَعَلَيْهِ قَضَاؤُهُ وَ قَالَ زُفَرُ يَتَادَى صَومَ رَمَضَانَ بِدُونِ الْبَيَةِ فِي حَقِّ الصَّحِيح الْمُقِيمِ لِأَنَّ الْإِمْسَاكَ مُسْتَحَقِّ عَلَيْهِ فَعَلَى أَي وَجْهِ يَوْدِّيْهِ يَقَعُ عَنْهُ كَمَا إِذَا وَهَبَ كُلَّ النَّصَابِ لِلْفَقِيرِ وَلَنَا أنَّ الْمُسْتَحَقَ الإمْسَاكُ بِجِهَةِ الْعِبَادَةِ وَ لَا عِبَادَةَ إِلَّا بِالنّيَةِ وَ فِى هِبَة البَصَابِ وَ جِدَ نِيَّةُ الْقُرْبَةِ عَلَى مَامَرَّ فِي الزَّكوة ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے پورے رمضان میں نہ روزے کی نیت کی اور نہ افطار کی نیت کی تو اس پر اس کی قضاء واجب ہے۔ اور امام زفر نے فرمایا ہے کہ رمضان کا روزہ تندرست مقیم کے حق میں بغیر نیت کے ادا ہو جاتا ہے کیونکہ کھانے ، پینے اور جماع سے رکے رہنا اس پر واجب ہے تو جس طریقہ پر اس کو ادا کرے گا اس کی طرف سے واقع ہو جائے گا۔ جیسے کسی نے پورا انصاب فقیر کو ہبہ کر دیا ہو۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ واجب وہ امساک ہے جو بہت عبادت ہو۔ اور عبادت بغیر نیت نہیں ہوتی ہے اور نصاب ہبہ کرنے کی صورت میں عبادت کی نیت پائی گئی ۔ جیسا کہ کتاب الزکوۃ میں گزر چکا۔
Whoever, during the entire Ramadan, neither intended fasting nor intended breaking fast, then qada' is obligatory upon him. And Zufar said: The fast of Ramadan is performed without intention in the case of a healthy resident, because abstention (imsak) is obligatory upon him, so in whatever manner he performs it, it occurs on his behalf, just as if someone gave the entire nisab to a poor person. And our proof is that what is obligatory is abstention (imsak) as an act of worship, and there is no worship without intention. And in the case of giving the nisab as charity, the intention of closeness to Allah is present, as has passed in the book of Zakat. Translation: And whoever, during the entire Ramadan, neither intended fasting nor intended breaking fast, then qada' is obligatory upon him. And Imam Zufar said that the fast of Ramadan is performed without intention in the case of a healthy resident, because refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse is obligatory upon him, so in whatever manner he performs it, it is valid on his behalf. Just as if someone gave the entire nisab to a poor person. And our proof is that what is obligatory is the abstention which is an act of worship, and worship is not valid without intention, and in the case of giving the nisab as charity, the intention of worship was present, as has passed in the book of Zakat.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about a healthy resident who, for the entire month of Ramadan, neither forms the intention to fast nor the intention to break fast (i.e., he refrains from eating, drinking, and intercourse without any specific intention). The Hanafi position holds that such a person must make up (qada') all the fasts of Ramadan because his abstention lacked intention, and fasting is an act of worship that requires niyyah. Imam Zufar argues that for a healthy resident, the fast of Ramadan is valid even without explicit intention because the obligation of abstention (imsak) is established by the mere fact of being in Ramadan; thus any abstention from nullifiers counts as fulfilling the obligation. He analogizes to a person who, without intending charity, gives away his entire nisab (minimum wealth subject to zakat) to a poor person; the poor person's receipt fulfills the obligation of zakat because the wealth is due. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the imsak obligatory in Ramadan is specifically an act of worship ('ibadah), and all acts of worship require intention. The analogy with zakat fails because when one gives away the nisab, there is an implicit intention of worship (qurbah) as the act is inherently charitable. The passage ends by referencing a more detailed discussion in the book of Zakat.

Key Terms
نیت (niyyah): The intention to perform an act of worship, specifically here the intention to fast Ramadan. The text emphasizes its necessity for validity.
امساک (imsak): Refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse during the daylight hours of Ramadan. The text discusses whether this imsak alone, without intention, constitutes fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling for a healthy resident who, during the entire month of Ramadan, neither intends to fast nor intends to break fast (i.e., merely abstains without intention)?
A) His fast is valid based on the obligation of imsak.
B) He must make up (qada') the entire month.
C) He is excused because he did not intend to break fast.
D) Only the first day is valid; the rest require qada'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fasting day in Ramadan is invalid without intention before dawn, even if the person refrains from nullifiers.

Qada for Extended Insanity: Original vs Temporary
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada for insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi majority view and Imam Muhammad's view on the effect of original vs temporary insanity on qada of Ramadan fasts, and identify the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
Original insanity exempts from qada of past days even if recovery occurs; temporary insanity requires qada unless the entire month is insane.

The obligation to make up missed Ramadan fasts is a fard 'ayn. However, prolonged insanity waives this obligation due to hardship. In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, the author explains the duration criterion (one month) and then introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: does original insanity (before puberty) differ from temporary insanity (after puberty)? The majority (Zahir al-Riwayah) treat them equally, but Imam Muhammad distinguishes, and his view is preferred by later scholars. Let us trace the reasoning and its practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Understand that qada for missed Ramadan fasts is obligatory unless waived by prolonged insanity. The threshold for prolonged insanity in fasting is one full month. If insanity lasts less than a month, qada is required; if a month or more, qada is waived due to hardship (haraj).
This is the settled ruling from the passage.
2
Analyze the talil (reasoning)
The author analogizes insanity to childhood (prolonged state, waives qada) and fainting (brief, does not waive). Insanity can be either; it is judged by duration: if prolonged (≥1 month), it associates with childhood; if short, with fainting. This is the logical basis for the duration criterion.
The ta'lil establishes the general principle.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The author of Hidayah states no difference between original insanity (junun asli, before puberty) and temporary insanity (junun 'aridi, after puberty). Both are judged solely by duration. However, Imam Muhammad distinguishes: original insanity follows the ruling of childhood — if recovery occurs during Ramadan, past days are not qada. Temporary insanity follows the ruling of fainting — qada for past days is required unless the entire month is insane.
Imam Muhammad's view uses the nature of the insanity, not just duration.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For a person with original insanity who recovers during Ramadan (e.g., on day 15), according to Imam Muhammad, no qada for days before recovery. According to the majority, if the total insane duration is less than a month, qada is required for those days. The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) prefer Imam Muhammad's view, so in fatwa, the distinction is often applied.
The mufta bihi is Imam Muhammad's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم

So if it is prolonged, it associates with non-bulugh; if not prolonged, it associates with fainting. But in the case of temporary insanity, qada of past days becomes obligatory. The difference stated by Imam Muhammad is the preferred madhhab of some later scholars. Benefit: Original insanity is that a person was insane before puberty. Temporary insanity is that he was mature and sane, then later became insane.

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of insanity (junun) on the obligation to make up missed Ramadan fasts. The author clarifies that if insanity lasts less than one month, qada is obligatory because there is no unbearable hardship. If it lasts a full month or more, qada is waived due to hardship (haraj). The reasoning draws an analogy with fainting (ighma) which is brief and does not waive qada, and childhood (adam bulugh) which is prolonged and does waive qada. Insanity, being possibly either, is judged by duration: a month sets it apart as prolonged. The passage then notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Zahir al-Riwayah, there is no difference between original insanity (junun asli, before puberty) and temporary insanity (junun 'aridi, after maturity). Imam Muhammad, however, distinguishes: original insanity follows the ruling of childhood—if the person recovers during Ramadan, qada of past days is not required. Temporary insanity requires qada if there is recovery. This view is preferred by some later scholars (mutakhkhirin). The commentary emphasizes that the criterion is the duration and nature of the insanity.

Key Terms
جنون (junun): Insanity that removes legal responsibility, divided into original (asli, before puberty) and temporary ('aridi, after maturity).
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory fast after Ramadan.
حَرَج (haraj): Hardship that warrants a concession in Islamic law; the basis for waiving qada in prolonged insanity.

Qada for Abstention Without Intention in Ramadan
Clarifying obligation of qada when one abstains without intending fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on qada for a healthy resident who abstains from mufattirat without intention, and evaluate Imam Zufar's opposing view.

Core Idea
Intention is necessary for a valid fast; mere abstention does not suffice — qada is wajib.

When someone refrains from eating and drinking during Ramadan but does not intend to fast, does that count as a valid fast? In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Zufar on whether qada is required. The central issue is the role of intention (niyyah) in worship. Understanding this debate clarifies a fundamental principle of Hanafi fiqh: that physical abstention must be coupled with the intention of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that qada is wajib for a healthy resident who abstains from mufattirat without intention. Imam Zufar holds that qada is not wajib because the fast is valid without intention.
Both agree that for a traveler or sick person, qada is wajib by consensus.
2
Understand the common ground
The disagreement only applies to a healthy, resident person. For a traveler or sick person, qada is wajib by agreement.
3
Analyze Zufar's proof (qiyas with zakat)
Imam Zufar argues that since abstention from mufattirat is wajib on a Ramadan day, any abstention fulfills the wajib, just as giving the nisab of zakat fulfills zakat even without explicit intention.
The analogy relies on the idea that the physical act alone suffices for obligation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (distinguishing the analogy)
The Hanafi school responds that refraining from mufattirat is only wajib as an act of worship; without intention, it is not worship. In zakat, the giver inherently intends reward (thawab), implying the intention of worship. Here, no such intention exists.
Thus the analogy fails because the zakat case already has implicit intention.
5
State the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's: qada is wajib. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that the person must make up that day.
If Zufar's view were followed, no qada would be required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص رمضان کے دن میں مفطرات صوم سے رکا رہا لیکن اس نے نہ روزے کی نیت کی اور نہ افطار کی نیت کی تو شخص اگر مسافر یا پیار ہوتو بالا اتفاق قضاء واجب ہے اور اگر تندرست اور مقیم ہوتو ہمارے نزدیک قضاء واجب ہے لیکن امام زفر کے نزدیک قضاء واجب نہیں ہے۔ حضرت امام زفر کامذہب یہ ہے کہ رمضان کا روزہ تندرست مقیم کے حق میں بغیر نیت صوم کے بھی ادا ہو جاتا ہے۔ امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رمضان کے دن میں کھانے پینے اور جماع سے رکنا واجب ہے لہذا جس طرح بھی اس واجب کو ادا زفر کرے گا ادا ہو جائے گا۔ خواہ نیت کرے یا نیت نہ کرے۔ جیسے کسی شخص نے مال نصاب پر سال گذرنے کے بعد پورا نصاب کسی فقیر کو ہیہ کر دیا اور زکوۃ ادا کرنے کی نیت نہیں کی تو زکوۃ ادا ہو جائیگی اس طرح رمضان کے دن میں اگر بغیر نیت کے امساک عن المفطرات پایا گیا تو اس سے رمضان کا روزہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ رمضان کے دن میں کھانے پینے اور جماع سے مطلقاً رکنا واجب نہیں ہے بلکہ بطریق عبادت رکنا واجب ہے اور بغیر نیت کے کھانے پینے اور جماع سے رکنا عبادت نہیں ہوتا اور یہاں مسئلہ یہ فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ اس نے نیت نہیں کی تو اس سے لازم آیا کہ اس نے صوم عبادت ادا نہیں کیا اور جب صوم عبادت ادا نہیں کیا تو اس پر قضاء واجب ہوگی۔ اور رہا پورے نصاب کو ہبہ کرنا تو اس میں عبادت کی نیت پائی گئی بائیں طور کہ اس نے ثواب حاصل کرنے کے ارادہ سے فقیر کو مال دیا ہے پس جب عبادت کی نیت پائی گئی تو زکوۃ بھی ادا ہوگئی ہے تفصیل کتاب الزکوۃ میں گذر چکی ہے۔
Explanation of the issue: If a person refrained from things that break the fast during a day of Ramadan, but he neither intended to fast nor intended to break the fast, then if the person is a traveler or sick, by agreement qada is obligatory. If he is healthy and resident, in our view qada is obligatory, but according to Imam Zufar, qada is not obligatory. Imam Zufar's school is that the fast of Ramadan for a healthy resident is valid even without the intention of fasting. Imam Zufar's proof: refraining from eating, drinking, and intercourse is obligatory on a day of Ramadan, so however he fulfills this obligation, it will be fulfilled, whether he intends or not. For example, if someone gave the entire nisab to a poor person after a year passed and did not intend zakat, zakat is still fulfilled. Similarly, if abstention from mufattirat occurred without intention, the Ramadan fast is valid. Our proof: refraining absolutely from eating, drinking, and intercourse on a day of Ramadan is not obligatory; rather, refraining in the manner of worship is obligatory. Abstention without intention is not worship. Here we have assumed he did not intend, so it follows that he did not perform the fast as worship. When he did not perform the worshipful fast, qada is obligatory. As for giving the entire nisab as a gift, there was the intention of worship found therein, because he gave money to the poor with the intention of reward. So when the intention of worship was found, zakat was fulfilled. The detail has passed in the Book of Zakat.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of qada for a healthy resident who, during a Ramadan day, refrains from everything that breaks the fast but does so without intending to fast. Imam Abu Hanifa (our school) holds that qada is obligatory because a valid fast requires intention (niyyah). Imam Zufar, however, holds that the mere abstention on a Ramadan day suffices to fulfill the fast, drawing an analogy to zakat where giving the nisab fulfills the obligation even without explicit intention. The Hanafi response refutes this by distinguishing between mere abstention and worshipful abstention. In the zakat analogy, the intention is implicitly present because the giver seeks reward (thawab). In the case of fasting, the person did not intend worship at all, so the fast is invalid and qada is required. The authority weight of this discussion is from the later commentary (sharh) on Hidayah, which is based on Zahir al-Riwayah positions.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship after its prescribed time.
نیت (niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform a specific act of worship.
امساک عن المفطرات (imsāk ʿani l-mufaṭṭirāt): Refraining from things that break the fast (eating, drinking, intercourse).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A healthy resident did not intend to fast at dawn but refrained from all mufattirat until sunset. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is valid and no qada is required.
B) Qada is wajib because intention is necessary.
C) Kaffarah is wajib for breaking the fast.
D) No action is required because he did not intend to fast.

Key Takeaway
Intention (niyyah) is essential for a valid fast; without it, mere abstention does not fulfill the obligation, and qada is required.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Kaffarah When No Intention to Fast: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Examining obligation of kaffarah when one eats after not intending to fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on kaffarah for a person who did not intend to fast in Ramadan and then ate, explain the reasoning of each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
No valid fast exists without intention, so eating does not incur kaffarah—only qada is required.

This passage examines a nuanced scenario: a person in Ramadan begins the day without intending to fast, then eats. Is kaffarah (expiation) obligatory? The Hanafi school has three distinct positions, with the relied-upon view being that of Imam Abu Hanifa. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how intention (niyyah) is fundamental to the validity of fasting and how legal analogies (qiyas) are applied within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the three positions
The act is eating during Ramadan without having intended to fast at dawn. The three Hanafi positions are: Abu Hanifa (no kaffarah), Zufar (kaffarah always), and the Sahibayn (kaffarah only if eating before zawal).
The Sahibayn are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Analyze each position's proof (dalil)
Abu Hanifa argues that kaffarah is only for breaking a valid fast, and no fast exists without intention. Zufar says the fast is valid without intention, so breaking it incurs kaffarah. The Sahibayn say eliminating the possibility of fasting (fawt imkan al-tahsil) before zawal is like breaking the fast, using the analogy of ghāsib al-ghāsib.
The Sahibayn's analogy is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, only qada (make-up) is required; under Zufar, both qada and kaffarah; under Sahibayn, qada always, and kaffarah only if eating before zawal.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, so in practice no kaffarah.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preference) mechanism
The commentary concludes that Abu Hanifa's view is relied upon because intention is a necessary condition for the validity of fasting. Without intention, there is no fast to break.
This reflects the Hanafi usul principle that a ruling's cause (sabab) must be present.

One who started the morning without intending to fast, then ate: is kaffarah due on him or not? Statements of jurists. And he who started the morning not intending to fast, then ate—there is no kaffarah upon him according to Abu Hanifa. Zufar said: Kaffarah is due upon him because in his view the fast is valid without intention. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: If he ate before zawal, kaffarah becomes obligatory, because he eliminated the possibility of achieving the fast, so he becomes like the usurper of what was usurped. For Abu Hanifa: Kaffarah is attached to breaking (ifsad), and this is merely abstaining, since there is no fast except with intention. Translation: And the person who started the morning not intending to fast, then ate something—Imam Abu Hanifa holds that kaffarah is not wajib. Imam Zufar said that kaffarah is wajib because in his view the fast is valid without intention. The Sahibayn said that if he ate before zawal, kaffarah becomes wajib because he eliminated the possibility of acquiring the fast, so he becomes like the usurper of the usurped. Abu Hanifa's proof: Kaffarah is attached to breaking the fast, and this person merely refrained from fasting, because a fast only exists with intention. Explanation: The scenario is that a person started the morning in Ramadan without intending to fast, then he ate or drank something before or after zawal. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah is due, but qada is wajib. According to Imam Zufar, both qada and kaffarah are wajib. The Sahibayn said: If he broke the fast before zawal, kaffarah is wajib; if after zawal, no kaffarah. Imam Zufar's proof: In his view, the Ramadan fast is valid without intention, so it is said that he started the day in a state of fasting, then he intentionally broke that fast; intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast makes kaffarah wajib. So in this case too, kaffarah is wajib, exactly as if he had intended and then broke. The Sahibayn's proof: It was possible to make the intention before zawal, but by eating before zawal he eliminated that possibility. Eliminating the possibility of obtaining the fast is like breaking a fast. And breaking a fast incurs kaffarah. This is like the usurper of what was usurped (ghāsib al-ghāsib): For example, Zayd usurped a thing from Khalid, so Zayd is obliged to return it. But he did not return it until Hamid took it from Zayd and destroyed it. Hamid eliminated the possibility of returning the usurped item. Thus, just as Khalid has the right to take compensation from Zayd the usurper, he also can take compensation from Hamid the usurper-of-the-usurper. This is not because Hamid usurped from Khalid directly, but because he eliminated the possibility of return. Eliminating the possibility of something is like eliminating the thing itself. Therefore, Hamid must pay compensation. Similarly, by eating before zawal, the person eliminated the possibility of obtaining the fast, which is tantamount to breaking it, so kaffarah is wajib. For eating after zawal, since the time for intention has passed, possibility was not lost, so no kaffarah. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: Kaffarah becomes wajib due to intentionally breaking a fast, and breaking requires a fast to exist. A fast does not exist without intention. In the given scenario, no fast existed because he did not intend. Therefore, there is no act of breaking. At most, he refrained from fasting. It is well known that kaffarah is due for breaking a fast, not for not fasting. So kaffarah is not wajib in this case.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of kaffarah for a person who did not intend to fast in Ramadan but then ate. Three positions emerge: Imam Abu Hanifa holds no kaffarah because there was no valid fast to break—only qada is required. Imam Zufar holds both qada and kaffarah, since he considers the fast valid without intention. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) distinguish based on time: if he ate before zawal, kaffarah is wajib because he could have intended and fasted; if after zawal, no kaffarah. The Sahibayn's analogy to ghāsib al-ghāsib is explained in detail: just as one who eliminates the possibility of returning a usurped item is liable for compensation, so too one who eliminates the possibility of fasting becomes liable for kaffarah. The commentary clarifies that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, because intention is fundamental to the validity of fasting.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): An expiatory penalty required for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse. In the Hanafi school, it is fasting 60 consecutive days or feeding 60 poor people.
غاصب الغاصب (ghāsib al-ghāsib): One who usurps from a usurper, i.e., takes an already usurped item. Used in legal analogy for liability based on eliminating the possibility of restitution.
نية (niyyah): Intention, a necessary condition (shart) for the validity of fasting according to the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, a person who did not intend to fast at dawn and then eats before zawal is obligated to:
A) Neither qada nor kaffarah
B) Qada only
C) Kaffarah only
D) Both qada and kaffarah

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, without intention there is no valid fast, so eating does not incur kaffarah—only qada is required; the positions of Zufar and the Sahibayn are not relied upon in practice.

Women in Hayd/Nifas: Break Fast and Make Up Later
Ruling that menstruating women break the fast and make up days, unlike prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a menstruating or postpartum woman regarding fasting, explain why it differs from prayer, and apply the rational basis to similar cases.

Core Idea
Missed fasts due to hayḍ/nifās must be made up; missed prayers are waived because of the hardship of making up many prayers.

This lesson addresses a clear distinction in Islamic law: while both fasting and prayer are invalidated by menstruation or postpartum bleeding, the obligation to make up missed acts differs. The Ḥanafī school, following the lead of the Qur’ān and Sunnah, requires making up missed fasts but completely waives missed prayers. The reason lies in the frequency of prayers versus the limited number of fasts. Let us examine the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is fasting during Ramadan for a woman in a state of hayḍ (menstruation) or nifās (postpartum bleeding). The ruling: she must break the fast (タらない) and later make up (qaḍā) the missed days.
The ruling is specific to obligatory Ramadan fasts; voluntary fasts are also prohibited during these states but do not require qaḍā.
2
Locate the proof (dalīl)
The text provides a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī): making up many prayers would cause hardship (ḥaraj), whereas the number of missed fasts is small (around 5–10 days per month). This rational justification distinguishes the ruling from prayer.
The proof is not a direct nass but a derived rationale; it is considered zannī in transmission but sound in meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
How does the proof connect to the ruling? The hardship of making up a month's worth of prayers (approximately 30 days × 5 prayers = 150 prayers) is excessive, so the Lawgiver waived qaḍā entirely. For fasting, the missed days are few (typically 5–7), making qaḍā manageable without undue hardship.
This reasoning is consistent with the principle of rafʿ al-ḥaraj (removal of hardship) in Islamic law.
4
Note the uniqueness and scope
The ruling is unanimous within the Ḥanafī school and agreed upon across schools (muttafaq ʿalayh). It applies specifically to obligatory Ramadan fasts. Voluntary fasts broken due to hayḍ/nifās are not required to be made up.
The text references an earlier discussion on prayer to contrast the two acts.

Kaffarah for Eating Without Intention Before Noon
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kaffarah for eating before noon without prior intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on kaffarah for eating before noon without intention, and evaluate the reasoning of each side.

Core Idea
Kaffarah requires a pre-existing fast; without intention, no fast exists to break.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute. The issue arises when a person eats during Ramadan before noon without having made the intention for the fast. While both sides agree the act is sinful, they differ on whether kaffarah applies. We will explore the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key legal question
What is the factual situation described in the text? What legal ruling is being disputed?
2
Follow the Sahibayn's argument
How do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani derive the obligation of kaffarah? What is the role of imkan al-sawm?
3
Examine the ghasib al-ghasib analogy
Why do the Sahibayn analogize to the second usurper? How does the concept of 'eliminating possibility' justify liability?
4
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa reject the analogy? What is the fundamental requirement for kaffarah?
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the practical outcome
Which position is relied upon in the Hanafi school? What is the practical difference in ruling?

روزہ پہلے سے موجود ہو اور روزہ بغیر نیت کے موجود نہیں ہوتا ۔ پس صورت مذکورہ فی المتن میں روزہ کی نیت نہ کرنے کی وجہ سے روزہ موجود ہی نہیں ہوا۔ اور جب روزہ موجود نہیں ہوا تو اس کا توڑنا کہاں سے ہو گا۔ زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ یہ شخص روزہ رکھنے سے رک گیا۔ اور یہ بات سب ہی کو معلوم ہے کہ کفارہ روزہ توڑنے سے واجب ہوتا ہے روزہ نہ رکھنے سے کفارہ واجب نہیں ہوتا ، ثابت ہوا کہ اس صورت میں کفارہ واجب نہ ہوگا۔
The reasoning of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) is that it was possible to make the intention and fast before noon, but when he ate or drank before noon, he eliminated the possibility of fasting. And eliminating the possibility of performing the fast is like having broken the fast after keeping it. And breaking a fast after keeping it necessitates kaffarah, so in this case also kaffarah becomes obligatory. And this is analogous to the usurper of a usurper. For example, Zayd usurped something from Khalid, so Zayd is obliged to return the usurped item, but he did not return it until Hamid usurped that item from Zayd and destroyed it. Then Hamid eliminated the possibility of returning that usurped item. So just as Khalid has the right to take compensation from Zayd the usurper, similarly he has the right to take compensation from Hamid the usurper of the usurper, but not because Hamid usurped this item from Khalid, but because Hamid eliminated the possibility of returning the item to its owner. And eliminating the possibility of a thing is like eliminating the thing itself, so it is as if Hamid usurped an item from Khalid and eliminated it, and if a usurper eliminates a usurped item, compensation becomes obligatory upon him, so compensation can be made obligatory upon Hamid the usurper of the usurper. So similarly, by eating and drinking before noon, that person eliminated the possibility of fasting, and eliminating the possibility of fasting is in reality eliminating the fast, and kaffarah becomes obligatory due to eliminating and breaking the fast, so in the case of breaking the fast before noon, kaffarah becomes obligatory. And in the case of breaking the fast after noon, since eliminating the possibility of fasting is not necessarily entailed, because after noon is not the time for intention of fasting, so in the case of breaking the fast after noon, kaffarah will not be obligatory. The reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa is that kaffarah becomes obligatory due to deliberately breaking a fast, and breaking a fast requires that the fast existed beforehand, and a fast does not exist without intention. So in the case mentioned in the text, since he did not make the intention for fasting, the fast did not exist at all. And when the fast did not exist, how can it be broken? The most that can be said is that this person refrained from fasting. And everyone knows that kaffarah is obligatory due to breaking a fast, not due to not fasting. So it is proven that in this case kaffarah is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a person who, during Ramadan, eats or drinks before noon without having made an intention for the fast. The key issue is whether kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory upon such a person. The Sahibayn argue that because it was possible to intend the fast before noon, by eating he has eliminated the possibility (imkan) of fasting, which is analogous to breaking an existing fast. They draw an analogy (qiyas) with the concept of 'ghasib al-ghasib' (usurper of a usurper), where the second usurper is liable because he eliminates the possibility of returning the item, not because he directly wronged the owner. Thus they hold kaffarah wajib. Imam Abu Hanifa counters that kaffarah is only for breaking a valid fast; a valid fast requires an intention made before dawn (for Ramadan) or before noon (for other fasts). Since no intention existed, there was no fast to break. Therefore, he holds that no kaffarah is required, though the person is sinful for eating in Ramadan. The Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that kaffarah is not obligatory in this case, as it follows the principle that kaffarah is tied to breaking an existing fast, not merely to the act of eating in Ramadan.

Key Terms
امکان الصوم (imkan al-sawm): The legal possibility of fasting on a given day, which exists before noon if one has not yet eaten or drunk.
غاصب الغاصب (ghasib al-ghasib): A second usurper who takes property from the first usurper, destroying the possibility of return to the original owner.
كفارة (kaffarah): An expiatory penalty for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast, consisting of freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is kaffarah not obligatory for a person who eats before noon without having made an intention for the Ramadan fast?
A) Because he can still make the intention after noon.
B) Because kaffarah is only for breaking a fast, and a fast does not exist without intention.
C) Because he is a traveler and fasting is not obligatory.
D) Because he ate forgetfully.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, kaffarah is tied to breaking a valid fast; without intention, no fast exists, so no kaffarah is incurred, though qada is required.

Ruling for Menstruating and Postpartum Women: Break Fast and Make Up Later
Ruling with comparison to prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a woman in hayd or nifas must break her fast and make it up, and explain why prayer is not made up.

Core Idea
Fasting missed due to menstruation must be made up; prayer is exempt because of its frequency and hardship.

In this lesson, we examine the clear Hanafi ruling that a woman experiencing menstrual or postpartum bleeding during Ramadan breaks her fast and later makes up those days. This is contrasted with prayer, which is entirely excused from makeup due to the principle of hardship. We will explore the underlying reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the exception
Fasting during Ramadan is obligatory for every adult Muslim, but women in a state of hayd (menstruation) or nifas (postpartum bleeding) are prohibited from fasting. If such bleeding begins while fasting, the fast becomes invalid and must be broken immediately.
2
Note the consequence: qada' is required
After the bleeding stops and the woman becomes pure, she must make up (qada') the missed fasts any day before the next Ramadan. This is wajib (obligatory) upon her.
Qada' of fasts is distinct from prayer: there is no requirement to pray them in order, but the fasts must be compensated one-for-one.
3
Compare with prayer
Prayers missed during hayd or nifas are not required to be made up. The passage states 'unlike prayer' and explains that making up numerous prayers each month would cause excessive hardship (haraj).
4
Understand the rationale
The Hanafi school applies the maxim 'raf' al-haraj' (removal of hardship). Prayers occur five times daily, and women experience hayd about once a month; requiring makeup of dozens of prayers would be burdensome. Fasting occurs only once a year, so makeup of a few days is manageable.
This reasoning is based on qiyas and istihsan, not a direct text.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
If a woman begins bleeding during Ramadan, she stops fasting immediately. After Ramadan, she calculates the days of hayd/nifas that occurred during Ramadan and fasts that many days. She does not perform any makeup for missed prayers.
For nifas, the maximum period is 40 days in the Hanafi school; any bleeding beyond that is istihadah and does not break the fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And when a woman menstruates or is in postpartum bleeding, she breaks her fast and makes it up (qada), unlike prayer, because she would experience hardship in making it up. And this has passed in the section on prayer. Explanation: The ruling is that if a woman begins to see menstrual blood or gives birth during Ramadan, her ruling is that she should not fast during menstruation and postpartum, and she should make up those fasts after Ramadan, unlike prayer. That is, due to menstruation and postpartum, the makeup of fasts is not dropped; rather, the makeup of prayer is dropped. The reason for the difference is that due to the frequency of prayers and being affected by menstruation every month, the makeup of prayers was dropped, whereas fasting occurs only once a year, so there is no hardship in making up the fasts. Therefore, the makeup of fasts is retained; it was not dropped due to menstruation and postpartum.

Commentary
This passage states the well-established Hanafi ruling that a woman who experiences menstruation (hayd) or postpartum bleeding (nifas) during Ramadan must break her fast (since fasting is invalid in that state) and subsequently make up those missed days after Ramadan. This is contrasted with prayer (salah), which is not made up because of the hardship (haraj) of making up numerous prayers missed over several days each month. Fasting is only once a year, so the burden of makeup is minimal. The underlying principle is removal of hardship (raf' al-haraj), a key maxim in Hanafi fiqh. The text notes that the discussion on prayer has already passed earlier in the book.

Key Terms
حائض (ha'id): A woman experiencing menstrual bleeding, during which fasting is prohibited and invalid.
نفساء (nufasa'): A woman experiencing postpartum bleeding (nifas), which has the same ruling as hayd regarding fasting.
قضاء (qada'): The act of performing a missed religious obligation after its prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Women must make up missed Ramadan fasts due to hayd or nifas, but not prayers, based on the principle of hardship.

Imsak for the Traveler and Menstruating Woman: A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on imsak obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on imsak for a traveler arriving during the day, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Imsak is a duty owed to the venerated time of Ramadan, not a substitute for a missed fast.

When a traveler arrives home mid-day in Ramadan, or a menstruating woman becomes pure, must they abstain from food and drink for the rest of the day? This lesson examines the difference between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on this question. Understanding the underlying principles — the sanctity of time versus the concept of substitution — will sharpen your ability to analyze fiqh disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Note the two cases: a traveler arriving during the day, and a menstruating woman becoming pure during the day. Both were not obliged to fast from dawn.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that imsak (refraining) is wajib for them for the remainder of the day.
3
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that imsak is not wajib but mustahabb (recommended). They may eat if they wish.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Shafi'i argues that imsak is a substitute (khalaf) for the full fast. Since the original fast was not obligatory on them at dawn, the substitute is also not obligatory.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi rebuts by saying imsak is not a substitute but a direct obligation due to the sanctity of the time (haqq al-waqt). Ramadan is a venerated time; anyone who witnesses part of it must honor it, except those with a persistent excuse (udhr).
The exception: if the excuse (travel, menstruation, sickness) existed from the beginning of the day, imsak is not required even by Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَدِمَ الْمُسَافِرُ أَوْ طَهُرَتِ الحَائِضُ فِى بَعْضِ النَّهَارِ أَمْسَكَا بَقِيَّةَ يَوْمِهمَا وَ قَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ لَا يَجِبُ الْإِمْسَاك وَ عَلى هَذَا الْخِلَافِ كُلُّ مَنْ صَارَ أَهْلًا لِلْرُوْمِ وَلَمْ يَكُنْ كَذَلِكَ فِى أوَّلِ الْيَوْمِ هُوَ يَقُولُ التّشبيه خَلْفٌ فَلَا يَجِبُ إلَّا عَلَى مَنْ يَتَحَقَّقُ الْأَصْلُ فِى حَقَّهِ كَالْمُفْطِرِ مُتَعَمِّدًا أَوْ مُخْطِيًا وَلَنَا أنَّهُ وَجَبَ فَضَاء لِحَقِّ الْوَقْتِ لَا خَلْفًا لأنَّه وَقَتْ مُعَظَّمُ بِخِلَافِ الحَائِضِ والفَسَاءِ وَالْمَرِيضِ وَالْمُسَافِرٍ حَيْثُ لَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِمْ حَالَ قِيَامِ هَذِهِ الْأَعْدَارِ لِتَحَقَّقِ الْمَانِعَ عَنِ التَشَبِهِ حَسْبَ تَحقِّقِه عَنِ الصّوم
And when a traveler arrives or a menstruating woman becomes pure during some part of the day, they both must refrain (imsak) for the remainder of their day. And al-Shafi'i said: Imsak is not obligatory. And this difference applies to everyone who becomes eligible for fasting and was not like that at the beginning of the day. He (al-Shafi'i) says: Imitating (the fasting person) is a substitute (khalaf), so it is only obligatory upon one for whom the original (asl) is established, like one who breaks his fast deliberately or by mistake. Our (Hanafi) proof is that it (imsak) is obligatory as a fulfillment of the right of the time, not as a substitute, because the time (i.e., the day of Ramadan) is respected. Unlike the menstruating woman, the postpartum woman, the sick, and the traveler, where these excuses exist, imsak is not obligatory upon them because the impediment (mani') to imitating is present just as the impediment to fasting is present.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The scenario: a traveler who arrives at his hometown during the day in Ramadan, or a menstruating woman who becomes pure during the day. According to the Hanafis, both must abstain from eating, drinking, etc. (imsak) for the rest of the day, even though they did not have a valid fast from dawn. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that imsak is not obligatory for them. The Shafi'i argument is that imsak is merely a substitute (khalaf) for the actual fast, and substitutes are only obligatory when the original (the actual fast) is obligatory upon the person. Since the traveler or menstruating woman was not obligated to fast from the start, the substitute is also not obligatory. In contrast, one who deliberately or mistakenly broke a fast (e.g., ate thinking it was still night) had an original obligation, so imsak remains wajib. The Hanafi response is that imsak is not a substitute but a duty owed to the time itself (haqq al-waqt). The day of Ramadan is a venerable time (waqt mu'azzam), so anyone who witnesses part of it must honor it by abstaining, even if not fully obligated. However, some individuals (temporarily excused, like the sick or traveler) are exempt from imsak as well, because their excuse (udhr) is present and prevents the obligation of both the fast and its imitation.

Key Terms
إمساك (imsak): Refraining from eating, drinking, and other fast-breaking acts during the remaining part of the day, without the full validity of a fast.
خلف (khalaf): Substitute; something that stands in place of an original obligation.
وقت معظم (waqt mu'azzam): A venerated time period (here, the day of Ramadan) that demands respect through abstinence even from those not fully obligated to fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is imsak wajib for a traveler arriving during the day?
A) Because the traveler must make up the fast for that day, so he must imitate fasting.
B) Because the day of Ramadan is a venerated time that demands abstention as a duty to the time.
C) Because the traveler intended to fast from the beginning.
D) Because the traveler is required to imitate other Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes imsak as a direct obligation to honor the sacred time of Ramadan, not as a substitute for fasting.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Obligation of Imsaak for One Who Becomes Able During the Day
Inter-madhhab dispute on obligatory abstention for remainder of day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that imsaak is wajib upon a traveler returning or a menstruating woman becoming pure during a Ramadan day, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by identifying the key difference in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Imsaak is not a substitute for fasting but a right of Ramadan's venerated time—this distinction drives the Hanafi ruling.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The scenario: a traveler returns home or a menstruating woman becomes pure during a Ramadan day. Did they have a valid fast? No. Must they still abstain from eating, drinking, and marital relations for the rest of the day? The Hanafi school says yes—this abstention (imsaak) is wajib. The Shafi'i school says it is not obligatory. The disagreement hinges on the legal nature of imsaak: is it a substitute for fasting or an independent obligation arising from the sanctity of Ramadan? Understanding this difference reveals a core Hanafi principle about the honor of time (haqq al-waqt).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
Identify what each school rules for a traveler returning or a menstruating woman becoming pure during a Ramadan day. The Hanafi school says imsaak is wajib; the Shafi'i school says it is not obligatory.
The difference applies to anyone who becomes eligible for fasting during the day, e.g., a convert, a child reaching puberty, or an insane person regaining sanity.
2
Understand the Shafi'i argument
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that imsaak is a substitute (khalīfah) for fasting. A substitute is only obligatory when the original (fasting) is obligatory. Since the traveler and menstruating woman were not obligated to fast, no substitute can be required.
He compares to one who intentionally or mistakenly breaks fast—imsaak is required there because fasting was originally obligatory for them.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects the characterization of imsaak as a substitute. They argue that abstention occurs in only part of the day while fasting is the whole day, so it cannot be a valid substitute. Instead, imsaak is an independent obligation arising from the sanctity of Ramadan (haqq al-waqt).
The Prophet's hadith about the virtue of Ramadan supports the idea that the time itself is venerated and must be honored.
4
Identify the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi school cites a hadith: 'Whoever performs a good deed in Ramadan is like one who performed an obligatory duty; whoever performs an obligatory duty in it is like one who performed seventy obligatory duties in other times.' This establishes the immense virtue of Ramadan, implying that the time demands respect even from those not fasting.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but it supports the principle of ta'zim al-waqt (veneration of time).
5
Apply the distinction to other cases
Consider others who become eligible during the day: an apostate who becomes Muslim, a child who reaches puberty, an insane person who regains sanity. According to the Hanafi school, all must abstain for the remainder of the day. According to the Shafi'i school, they need not. Also note that those with perpetual excuses (e.g., menstruating women, sick persons) are not required to abstain because the hindrance prevents both fasting and imitation.
For menstruating and post-natal women, imitating fasters is forbidden because their fasting itself is forbidden. For the sick and traveler, imposing imitation would defeat the purpose of the concession (to remove hardship).

ترجمہ اور جب مسافر آ گیا یا حائضہ پاک ہو گئی دن کے کسی حصہ میں تو وہ دونوں بقیہ دن رکے رہیں ۔ اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ امساک واجب نہیں ہے۔ اور اس اختلاف پر ہر وہ شخص ہے جو روز ولازم ہونے کا اہل ہو گیا۔ حالانکہ اول یوم میں ایسا نہیں تھا۔ امام شافعی کہتے ہیں کہ روزہ دار کی مشابہت روزہ کا خلیفہ ہے۔ پس خلیفہ واجب نہ ہوگا مگر اس شخص پر جس کے حق میں اصل یعنی صوم تحقیق ہے۔ جیسے عمد یا خطا افطار کرنے والا اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ امساک وقت کا حق ادا کرنے کے واسطے واجب ہوا ہے نہ کہ خلیفہ ہونے کی جہت سے کیونکہ رمضان کا دن ایک معظم وقت ہے ۔ برخلاف حائضہ ، نفساء، بیمار اور مسافر کے، کیونکہ ان اعزار کی موجودگی میں ان پر امساک واجب نہیں ہے کیونکہ روزہ دار کی مشابہت سے مانع موجود ہے جیسے روزہ سے مانع موجود ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی مسافر رمضان کے دن میں اپنے وطن واپس آیا یا حائضہ پاک ہوگئی تو ہمارے نزدیک ان دونوں پر دن کے باقی حصہ میں مفطرات سے امساک واجب ہے یعنی ان دونوں کا پہلے سے روزہ نہیں تھا مگر اس کے باوجود شام تک روزہ داروں کی طرح وقت گزاریں۔ حضرت امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ ان پر باقی دن کا امساک واجب نہیں ہے۔ یہ اختلاف ہر اس آدمی کے بارے میں ہے جو دن کے کسی حصہ میں روزہ کامل کا اہل ہو گیا۔ مثلاً کا فرمسلمان ہو گیا یا بچہ بالغ ہو گیا یا مجنون کو افاقہ ہوگیا تو ہمارے نزدیک ان پر دن کے باقی حصہ میں امساک واجب ہے لیکن امام شافعی کے نزدیک واجب نہیں ہے۔ حضرت امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے عورت پر اصل یعنی روزہ واجب نہیں ہے تو اس کا خلیفہ یعنی امساک کس طرح واجب ہوگا ۔ اور مثلا اگر کسی نے رمضان میں عمداً افطار کر دیا یا خطا افطار کر دیا ، با ایں طور پر یوم شک میں کچھ کھالیا پھر معلوم ہوا
